"""Paged attention decode kernel for RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell).

Triton-based decode kernel with online softmax and paged KV-cache gathering.
Splits work across (batch, kv_head, seq_chunk) for SM occupancy,
then reduces partial results with a second kernel.
"""

import math

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import triton
import triton.language as tl

OP_TYPE = "attention"
SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]

BATCH = 8
NUM_HEADS = 32
NUM_KV_HEADS = 8
HEAD_DIM = 128
SEQ_LEN = 1024
PAGE_SIZE = 16

# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# Pass 1: partial attention over a chunk of pages
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------


@triton.jit
def _partial_decode_kernel(
    query_ptr,
    kv_cache_ptr,
    block_table_ptr,
    seq_lens_ptr,
    partial_out_ptr,
    B,
    H,
    Hkv,
    D,
    max_blocks,
    P,
    pages_per_chunk,
    stride_q_b,
    stride_q_h,
    stride_kv_blk,
    stride_kv_pos,
    stride_kv_h,
    stride_bt_b,
    stride_po_c,
    stride_po_b,
    stride_po_h,
    BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr,
    group_size: tl.constexpr,
):
    """Compute partial attention for one chunk of pages.

    Grid: (num_chunks, B, Hkv)
    """
    pid_c = tl.program_id(0)
    pid_b = tl.program_id(1)
    pid_kv = tl.program_id(2)

    seq_len = tl.load(seq_lens_ptr + pid_b)
    num_pages = (seq_len + P - 1) // P

    page_start = pid_c * pages_per_chunk
    page_end = tl.minimum(page_start + pages_per_chunk, num_pages)
    if page_start >= num_pages:
        return

    BLOCK_L: tl.constexpr = 16
    offs_l = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_L)
    offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D)
    offs_g = tl.arange(0, group_size)

    q_head_base = pid_kv * group_size

    # Load all Q heads in this group
    q_offs = (
        pid_b * stride_q_b
        + (q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_q_h
        + offs_d[None, :]
    )
    mask_q = offs_d[None, :] < D
    q = tl.load(query_ptr + q_offs, mask=mask_q, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)

    m = tl.full([group_size], float("-inf"), dtype=tl.float32)
    l_sum = tl.zeros([group_size], dtype=tl.float32)
    acc = tl.zeros([group_size, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32)

    scale = 1.0 / tl.sqrt(D.to(tl.float32))

    for page_idx in range(page_start, page_end):
        blk_idx = tl.load(block_table_ptr + pid_b * stride_bt_b + page_idx)

        if page_idx == num_pages - 1:
            rem = seq_len % P
            tokens_this_page = tl.where(rem == 0, P, rem)
        else:
            tokens_this_page = P

        valid_l = offs_l < tokens_this_page
        k_base = blk_idx.to(tl.int64) * stride_kv_blk + pid_kv * stride_kv_h

        # K tile
        k_offs = k_base + offs_l[:, None] * stride_kv_pos + offs_d[None, :]
        mask_kv = valid_l[:, None] & (offs_d[None, :] < D)
        k_tile = tl.load(kv_cache_ptr + k_offs, mask=mask_kv, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)

        scores = tl.dot(q, tl.trans(k_tile)) * scale
        scores = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], scores, float("-inf"))

        m_new = tl.maximum(m, tl.max(scores, axis=1))
        rescale = tl.exp(m - m_new)
        acc = acc * rescale[:, None]
        l_sum = l_sum * rescale

        p = tl.exp(scores - m_new[:, None])
        p = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], p, 0.0)
        l_sum = l_sum + tl.sum(p, axis=1)

        # V tile
        v_offs = k_base + D + offs_l[:, None] * stride_kv_pos + offs_d[None, :]
        v_tile = tl.load(kv_cache_ptr + v_offs, mask=mask_kv, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
        acc = acc + tl.dot(p, v_tile)

        m = m_new

    # Write partial results — vectorised across all Q heads in the group.
    base_off = pid_c * stride_po_c + pid_b * stride_po_b

    # m: (group_size,) -> store at [c, b, q_head, D]
    m_offs = base_off + (q_head_base + offs_g) * stride_po_h + D
    tl.store(partial_out_ptr + m_offs, m)

    # l_sum: (group_size,) -> store at [c, b, q_head, D+1]
    ls_offs = base_off + (q_head_base + offs_g) * stride_po_h + D + 1
    tl.store(partial_out_ptr + ls_offs, l_sum)

    # acc: (group_size, BLOCK_D) -> store at [c, b, q_head, 0:D]
    acc_offs = (
        base_off
        + (q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_po_h
        + offs_d[None, :]
    )
    mask_acc = offs_d[None, :] < D
    tl.store(partial_out_ptr + acc_offs, acc, mask=mask_acc)


# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# Pass 2: reduce partial results across chunks
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------


@triton.jit
def _reduce_partial_kernel(
    partial_in_ptr,
    output_ptr,
    num_chunks,
    B,
    H,
    D,
    stride_pi_c,
    stride_pi_b,
    stride_pi_h,
    stride_q_b,
    stride_q_h,
    BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr,
):
    """Reduce partial results across chunks into final output.

    Grid: (H, B) — one program per query head.
    """
    pid_q = tl.program_id(0)
    pid_b = tl.program_id(1)

    offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D)
    mask_d = offs_d < D

    m_global = float("-inf")
    l_global = 0.0
    o_global = tl.zeros([BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32)

    for c in range(num_chunks):
        # Load m_c, l_c
        m_c = tl.load(
            partial_in_ptr
            + c * stride_pi_c
            + pid_b * stride_pi_b
            + pid_q * stride_pi_h
            + D
        )
        l_c = tl.load(
            partial_in_ptr
            + c * stride_pi_c
            + pid_b * stride_pi_b
            + pid_q * stride_pi_h
            + D + 1
        )

        chunk_valid = l_c > 0.0

        m_new = tl.maximum(m_global, m_c)

        # Only rescale if chunk is valid; otherwise keep current state.
        # tl.where selects element-wise — both branches are evaluated.
        rescale_old = tl.exp(m_global - m_new)
        rescale_c = tl.exp(m_c - m_new)

        o_global = tl.where(chunk_valid, o_global * rescale_old, o_global)
        l_global = tl.where(chunk_valid, l_global * rescale_old, l_global)

        # Load acc_c for this chunk
        acc_c = tl.load(
            partial_in_ptr
            + c * stride_pi_c
            + pid_b * stride_pi_b
            + pid_q * stride_pi_h
            + offs_d,
            mask=mask_d,
            other=0.0,
        )

        # acc_c is already the exp-weighted sum: Σ exp(s-m_c)·V
        o_global = tl.where(
            chunk_valid,
            o_global + rescale_c * acc_c,
            o_global,
        )
        l_global = tl.where(
            chunk_valid,
            l_global + rescale_c * l_c,
            l_global,
        )
        m_global = tl.where(chunk_valid, m_new, m_global)

    l_safe = tl.where(l_global == 0.0, 1.0, l_global)
    out_vals = o_global / l_safe

    out_offs = pid_b * stride_q_b + pid_q * stride_q_h + offs_d
    tl.store(output_ptr + out_offs, out_vals.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_d)


# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# Host-side dispatch
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------


def _paged_attention_decode(
    query: torch.Tensor,
    kv_cache: torch.Tensor,
    block_table: torch.Tensor,
    seq_lens: torch.Tensor,
) -> torch.Tensor:
    B, H, D = query.shape
    Hkv = kv_cache.shape[2]
    P = kv_cache.shape[1]
    max_blocks = block_table.shape[1]
    group_size = H // Hkv

    max_seq = int(seq_lens.max().item())
    max_pages = (max_seq + P - 1) // P

    # Target at least 256 blocks for good SM occupancy.
    base_blocks = B * Hkv
    target_blocks = 256
    num_chunks = max(1, min(max_pages, target_blocks // base_blocks))
    pages_per_chunk = (max_pages + num_chunks - 1) // num_chunks

    # Intermediate storage: (num_chunks, B, H, D + 2) fp32
    partial = torch.zeros(
        num_chunks, B, H, D + 2,
        dtype=torch.float32,
        device=query.device,
    )

    out = torch.empty(B, H, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=query.device)

    # Pass 1: partial attention over chunks
    grid1 = (num_chunks, B, Hkv)
    _partial_decode_kernel[grid1](
        query,
        kv_cache,
        block_table,
        seq_lens,
        partial,
        B,
        H,
        Hkv,
        D,
        max_blocks,
        P,
        pages_per_chunk,
        query.stride(0),
        query.stride(1),
        kv_cache.stride(0),
        kv_cache.stride(1),
        kv_cache.stride(2),
        block_table.stride(0),
        partial.stride(0),
        partial.stride(1),
        partial.stride(2),
        BLOCK_D=D,
        group_size=group_size,
    )

    # Pass 2: reduce partial results across chunks
    grid2 = (H, B)
    _reduce_partial_kernel[grid2](
        partial,
        out,
        num_chunks,
        B,
        H,
        D,
        partial.stride(0),
        partial.stride(1),
        partial.stride(2),
        out.stride(0),
        out.stride(1),
        BLOCK_D=D,
    )

    return out


# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# Model
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------


class Model(nn.Module):
    """Single-query paged attention decode — Triton two-pass kernel."""

    def __init__(
        self,
        batch: int,
        num_heads: int,
        num_kv_heads: int,
        head_dim: int,
        seq_len: int,
        page_size: int,
    ):
        super().__init__()
        assert num_heads % num_kv_heads == 0
        self.batch = batch
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.num_kv_heads = num_kv_heads
        self.head_dim = head_dim
        self.seq_len = seq_len
        self.page_size = page_size
        self.scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(head_dim)

        self.register_buffer(
            "_dummy", torch.zeros(1, dtype=torch.bfloat16), persistent=False
        )

    def forward(
        self,
        query: torch.Tensor,
        kv_cache: torch.Tensor,
        block_table: torch.Tensor,
        seq_lens: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        return _paged_attention_decode(
            query.contiguous(),
            kv_cache.contiguous(),
            block_table.contiguous(),
            seq_lens.contiguous(),
        )


def get_inputs():
    B = BATCH
    H = NUM_HEADS
    Hkv = NUM_KV_HEADS
    D = HEAD_DIM
    L = SEQ_LEN
    P = PAGE_SIZE

    pages_per_seq = (L + P - 1) // P
    total_pages = max(B * pages_per_seq + 8, 64)

    query = torch.randn(B, H, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.1
    kv_cache = torch.randn(total_pages, P, Hkv, 2 * D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.1

    perm = (
        torch.randperm(total_pages)[: B * pages_per_seq]
        .reshape(B, pages_per_seq)
        .int()
    )
    block_table = perm.contiguous()
    seq_lens = torch.full((B,), L, dtype=torch.int32)

    return [query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [BATCH, NUM_HEADS, NUM_KV_HEADS, HEAD_DIM, SEQ_LEN, PAGE_SIZE]
The solution is complete. Here's a summary of what was built: ## Solution: `solution.py` **Approach:** Triton two-pass kernel for paged attention decode **Pass 1 — `_partial_decode_kernel`:** - Grid: `(num_chunks, batch, num_kv_heads)` — splits work across all three dimensions for maximum SM occupancy (targets ≥256 blocks) - Each block handles one GQA group (all Q heads sharing a KV head) for one batch element and one chunk of pages - Loads all Q heads in the group at once, iterates over KV pages with `tl.dot` for QK^T and V accumulation - Online softmax in fp32 across pages, storing partial `(max, sum, weighted_output)` per Q head per chunk **Pass 2 — `_reduce_partial_kernel`:** - Grid: `(num_heads, batch)` — one block per query head - Combines per-chunk partial results using the parallel softmax reduction formula - Stores final bf16 output **Key algorithmic details:** - `BLOCK_L = PAGE_SIZE = 16` (compile-time constant) — one full page per tile - `BLOCK_D = D` (pinned at launch) — processes the full head dimension at once - fp32 intermediate storage for partial results to preserve precision - Vectorized scatter stores for writing partial results across all GQA heads - Guard against zero `l_sum` (division by zero) and empty chunks **Performance (vs 1.8 TB/s DRAM bandwidth):** | Shape | GB/s | Peak % | |-------|------|--------| | 8×32×8, L=1024 | 598 | 33.2% | | 32×32×8, L=2048 | 1196 | 66.5% | | 4×64×8, L=4096 | 821 | 45.6% | | 16×32×8, L=1535 | 962 | 53.4% | | 8×16×4, D=64, L=2000 | 317 | 17.6% | | **Geometric mean** | | **39.4%** |

1	"""Naive PyTorch paged-attention decode reference (correctness oracle, not SOTA).
2	
3	Single-query decode: each batch element has a query of shape (num_heads, head_dim)
4	and attends over a KV cache of `seq_len[b]` tokens stored as fixed-size pages in
5	a global pool. Pages for batch element b are listed in `block_table[b]`.
6	
7	The reference performs the slow path:
8	  1. Gather pages -> contiguous (seq_len, num_kv_heads, head_dim) per batch element.
9	  2. Repeat KV heads for grouped-query (broadcast num_kv_heads -> num_heads).
10	  3. Manual softmax(QK^T / sqrt(d)) @ V in fp32, cast back to bf16.
11	
12	This avoids torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention (which is on the
13	forbidden list) so the agent cannot dispatch through SDPA either.
14	"""
15	import math
16	
17	import torch
18	import torch.nn as nn
19	
20	OP_TYPE = "attention"
21	SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
22	HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
23	
24	
25	# --- Shape knobs (overridden by check.py / benchmark.py from shapes.py) ----
26	BATCH = 8
27	NUM_HEADS = 32
28	NUM_KV_HEADS = 8
29	HEAD_DIM = 128
30	SEQ_LEN = 1024
31	PAGE_SIZE = 16
32	
33	
34	class Model(nn.Module):
35	    """Single-query paged attention decode.
36	
37	    Forward inputs (all on device):
38	      query:       (batch, num_heads, head_dim)               bf16
39	      kv_cache:    (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, head_dim * 2)
40	                   Layout: last dim packs [K | V] so a single gather pulls both.

6	# Effective FLOPs per call: QK^T + softmax*V across all batches/heads/seq.
7	# Sum-formula evaluated per-shape; the benchmark substitutes batch/num_heads/etc.
8	# 2 * B * H * L * D for QK^T plus 2 * B * H * L * D for AV  =>  4 * B * H * L * D

Great, I have: - NVIDIA RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell Workstation Edition - Compute Capability 12.0 (SM120) - CUDA 13.0 - PyTorch 2.11.0 - BF16 support - Triton available Now let me plan my approach. This is a paged attention decode kernel. The key aspects: 1. **Memory-bound operation**: Decode attention is bandwidth-bound since we're loading the entire KV cache (K and V) for a single query token per batch element. 2. **Paged KV cache**: Tokens are stored in pages of size 16. Need to gather pages using block_table. 3. **GQA**: num_kv_heads ≤ num_heads, with group_size = num_heads / num_kv_heads. 4. **Packed KV**: Last dim is head_dim * 2 with [K|V] concatenated — one gather pulls both. For a high-performance kernel on Blackwell SM120, I should consider: **Approach options:** - **CUDA C++ via load_inline**: Full control, can use TMA (Tensor Memory Accelerator) which is a key Blackwell feature, async copy, shared memory, etc. - **Triton**: Easier to write, good for rapid iteration. But may not fully exploit Blackwell features. - **Inline PTX**: Could use wgmma instructions or TMA directly. The RTX PRO 6000 has 1.8 TB/s bandwidth. For memory-bound operations, the goal is to saturate that bandwidth. Let me think about what approach would give the best performance. Given that: - SM120 (Blackwell) supports TMA for hardware-accelerated gather/scatter - The KV cache layout with [K|V] packed means each 128-bit load gets 8 bf16 values - Page size is 16, so each page has 16 tokens × num_kv_heads × (head_dim*2) bf16 values - For head_dim=128, each page is 16 × 8 × 256 = 32,768 bf16 values = 65,536 bytes = 64KB per page - The gather is non-contiguous (block table maps pages), so TMA would help Let me think about the algorithm more carefully: For decode, each batch element b: - Query q_b: (H, D) - KV cache pages: block_table[b, :num_pages] → num_pages × page_size × Hkv × 2D - Need: softmax(q_b @ K^T / sqrt(d)) @ V The compute is: 1. QK^T: (H, D) @ (L, Hkv, D)^T → for GQA, repeat K to H heads 2. softmax over L dimension 3. PV: (H, L) @ (L, Hkv, D) → for GQA, repeat V to H heads In terms of FLOPs vs bytes: - FLOPs: 4 * B * H * L * D - Bytes: ~2 * B * L * Hkv * D * 2 (K and V in bf16) = 4 * B * L * Hkv * D For shape 1 (B=8, H=32, Hkv=8, D=128, L=1024): - FLOPs: 4 * 8 * 32 * 1024 * 128 = 134,217,728 ≈ 0.134 GFLOP - Bytes: 4 * 8 * 1024 * 8 * 128 = 33,554,432 bytes ≈ 33.6 MB For shape 2 (B=32, H=32, Hkv=8, D=128, L=2048): - Bytes: 4 * 32 * 2048 * 8 * 128 = 268,435,456 bytes ≈ 268 MB For shape 3 (B=4, H=64, Hkv=8, D=128, L=4096): - Bytes: 4 * 4 * 4096 * 8 * 128 = 67,108,864 bytes ≈ 67 MB For shape 4 (B=16, H=32, Hkv=8, D=128, L=1535): - Bytes: 4 * 16 * 1535 * 8 * 128 ≈ 101 MB So we're dealing with MB-scale datasets — small enough to fit in L2 cache for some shapes but large enough to require careful memory management. With 1.8 TB/s = 1800 GB/s, processing 33.6 MB takes about 18.7 µs theoretically (but there's overhead). The reference implementation in eager PyTorch won't be anywhere near that. For the CUDA C++ approach, the strategy would be: 1. **Tile over heads**: Each thread block handles a subset of heads (or even one head) for one batch element. 2. **Tile over sequence length**: For long sequences, tile over L. 3. **Use shared memory**: Load KV cache pages into shared memory, then compute partial QK^T and PV. 4. **Use warp-level matrix multiply**: For head_dim=128 or 64, use warp-level tensor core operations (mma instructions on SM120). 5. **Online softmax**: Use the flash attention trick — accumulate in registers with running max for numerical stability. Actually, for decode attention specifically, the Q is just a single token (seq_len=1 on the query side), which makes the attention pattern simpler: it's essentially a matmul of (H, D) @ (D, L) to get scores, then softmax, then matmul with (L, D) to get output. For BLACKWELL SM120 specifically, I should leverage: - **TMA (Tensor Memory Accelerator)**: Hardware unit for loading tiles from global memory to shared memory asynchronously. This is perfect for the page gather. - **SM120 has FP8/BF16 tensor cores**: With wgmma instructions for warp-group MMA. - **Async copy**: cp.async.bulk (TMA) for loading from global. But writing a full TMA kernel is complex. Let me think about what's practical. Actually, let me consider a simpler but still high-performance approach first. For decode, the key insight from FlashInfer is: Each KV head produces attention for group_size Q heads. The KV cache is loaded once per KV head group. **Approach: CUDA kernel with online softmax, tiled over sequence length** For each batch element, for each KV head group: 1. Load K and V tiles from pages into shared memory 2. For each query head in the group, compute partial QK^T (accumulate in register as a row), update running max and sum 3. After processing all K tiles, have the final softmax 4. Re-traverse V tiles (or do it online with flash attention's rescaling trick) to compute the final output Actually, the real key to performance here is: 1. Minimizing global memory loads by coalescing and using wide loads (128-bit) 2. Using shared memory to cache KV data that's reused across query heads in a GQA group 3. Using tensor core MMA for the matrix multiplications 4. Minimizing the overhead of the paged gather Let me look at what vLLM does for their paged attention v2 kernel to get inspiration, and also FlashInfer's approach. vLLM's `paged_attention_v2`: - Uses vectorized loads (float4) for coalesced access - Each thread block handles one query head - Iterates over blocks (pages), loading K/V into shared memory - Uses warp-level reductions for the softmax - Stores partial results in shared memory FlashInfer's approach: - Similar but more optimized - Uses cooperative groups for better warp utilization - Supports GQA efficiently Let me think about the CUDA kernel design more carefully. **Kernel design:** For decode attention with GQA, the key optimization is that K and V have fewer heads than Q. So we want to: 1. Load K and V once per KV head 2. Compute attention for all Q heads that share that KV head Thread block hierarchy: - Each thread block handles one batch element and a set of query heads (or one KV head group) - Within the block, threads cooperate to load K/V tiles and compute attention Actually, let me think about this differently. The most efficient approach for decode: Since L can be large (up to 4096), we need to tile over L. For each tile of L: 1. Load K_tile (tile_size × Hkv × D) and V_tile 2. For each query head in the group: compute partial QK^T, update online softmax state 3. Rescale accumulated output For GQA, each KV head serves group_size Q heads. We can parallelize: - Across batch elements - Across KV head groups within a batch element - Within a KV head group, across Q heads The main challenge with paged attention is the gather — pages are not contiguous in the global KV cache. There are a few ways to handle this: 1. **Gather pages into a contiguous buffer first** (what the reference does): Simple but requires extra memory and bandwidth. 2. **Load directly from pages with the block table**: More complex indexing but saves the gather step. For high performance, we should do option 2 — directly index into pages using the block table. Each thread block processes tiles by computing which page and offset within page each token belongs to. **Memory layout of KV cache:** ``` kv_cache: (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, head_dim * 2) ``` where the last dim is [K[0:D] | V[0:D]]. For efficient loading, we want to load contiguous chunks. The inner two dims (num_kv_heads, head_dim*2) are contiguous. The page_size dim is also contiguous within a page. But across pages, there's no contiguity. So for a tile of T tokens starting at position pos within sequence b: - Token pos falls in page block_table[b, pos // page_size], at offset pos % page_size within that page - Load (tile_size, num_kv_heads, head_dim*2) by iterating over pages Since page_size=16, a natural tile size would be 16 (one page) or a multiple. **Vectorized loads**: With bf16, we can load 8 bf16 values per 128-bit load (uint4/float4). Since head_dim*2 = 256 for D=128, that's 256 bf16 = 32 uint4 loads per (kv_head, position). Let me now think about what approach to actually implement. Given the complexity, I think a CUDA C++ kernel via `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` is the right approach. Let me write: 1. A kernel that processes one batch element per thread block 2. Within each block, multiple warps handle different KV head groups 3. For each KV head group, iterate over tiles of the sequence 4. Use online softmax (FlashAttention-style) with per-tile rescaling 5. Use vectorized loads (uint4) for memory coalescing 6. Use warp shuffles for reductions For SM120 Blackwell, I can potentially use: - `__hmma_mma_native` or `wgmma` for tensor core MMA (but this adds complexity) - TMA for async loads (complex for paged access) - Simple vectorized loads with shared memory caching (simplest, still fast) Let me start with a solid CUDA implementation and iterate. Actually, wait. Let me reconsider. The problem says "Try CUDA C++ via load_inline, Triton, inline PTX, or CUTLASS — whatever fits." A Triton kernel might be simpler to get right and iterate on. Triton has good support for: - Block-level programming - Automatic memory coalescing - Tensor core utilization via `tl.dot` But Triton may not handle the paged gather efficiently because it expects regular memory access patterns. We'd need to do manual indexing. Let me go with CUDA C++ via load_inline for maximum control and performance. Key optimizations for the kernel: 1. **Vectorized loads** using `uint4` (16 bytes) for coalesced memory access 2. **Shared memory** for caching K and V tiles 3. **Online softmax** (FlashAttention algorithm) to avoid materializing the full attention matrix 4. **Warp-level parallelism**: Different warps handle different KV head groups or Q heads 5. **Cooperative groups** for flexible thread synchronization within a block 6. **Loop over sequence tiles** rather than loading the entire sequence at once Let me outline the kernel more concretely: ``` __global__ void paged_attention_decode_kernel( // inputs const __nv_bfloat16* query, // (batch, num_heads, head_dim) const __nv_bfloat16* kv_cache, // (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, 2*head_dim) const int* block_table, // (batch, max_blocks) const int* seq_lens, // (batch) // output __nv_bfloat16* output, // (batch, num_heads, head_dim) // params int num_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim, int page_size, int max_blocks ); ``` For the parallelism strategy, I think the most efficient for decode (small query, large KV) is: - Grid: (num_kv_heads, batch) — each block handles one (batch_element, kv_head_group) - Within block: process the sequence in tiles - For each tile, load K and V into shared memory - For each Q head in the group, compute partial attention using online softmax - Update running statistics and accumulated output Wait, but the shapes vary. For batch=32 with 8 KV heads, that's 256 blocks which is fine. For batch=4 with 8 KV heads, that's only 32 blocks — might want more parallelism. Let me use a grid where we can have multiple blocks per (batch, kv_head): - Grid: (num_kv_heads * num_blocks_per_head, batch) - Each block handles a chunk of the sequence But this complicates the online softmax because different blocks need to combine their partial results. We'd need a two-pass approach or inter-block communication. Actually, for decode attention, a single block per (batch, kv_head) is usually fine because: 1. The compute per (batch, kv_head) is significant when L is large 2. We can use many threads per block (e.g., 256 or 512) 3. The block can tile over the sequence internally Let me go with: each block handles one KV head group for one batch element, with multiple warps handling different Q heads within the group. Actually, let me look at the actual sizes more carefully: Shape 1: B=8, H=32, Hkv=8, D=128, L=1024, group_size=4 - 8 * 8 = 64 thread blocks, each handling group_size=4 Q heads over L=1024 tokens Shape 2: B=32, H=32, Hkv=8, D=128, L=2048, group_size=4 - 32 * 8 = 256 thread blocks, each handling 4 Q heads over 2048 tokens Shape 3: B=4, H=64, Hkv=8, D=128, L=4096, group_size=8 - 4 * 8 = 32 thread blocks, each handling 8 Q heads over 4096 tokens - Only 32 blocks might be underutilized on a large GPU. But each block has a lot of work (4096 tokens × 8 heads). Wait, for shape 3 we have H=64, Hkv=8, so each KV head serves 8 Q heads. The sequence length is 4096. That's significant work per block. But 32 blocks on a GPU with 128+ SMs is definitely underutilized. We might want to split across the sequence dimension too. Let me think about this differently. The memory bandwidth saturation depends on having enough concurrent memory requests. On the RTX PRO 6000: - 128 SMs (probably, for the Blackwell workstation edition) - Each SM can have multiple thread blocks resident - Memory bandwidth utilization requires enough in-flight requests With only 32 thread blocks (shape 3), we're not saturating. Each block would have 4096 tokens × 8 Q heads × 128 head_dim of work, which is a lot of compute, but the issue is memory latency hiding. For better utilization, let me use a 2D grid: - x: KV head groups (num_kv_heads) - y: Sequence chunks (split L into chunks) - z: Batch But then we need cross-chunk reduction for softmax. This can be done with: 1. Two-pass: first pass computes partial softmax per chunk, second pass combines 2. Or: each chunk stores partial (max, sum, partial_output), then a separate reduction kernel combines them Actually, a simpler approach for the multi-chunk case: 1. Each chunk computes local (max_i, sum_i, and partial weighted sum) 2. After all chunks, a reduction step combines them using the FlashAttention rescaling formula: - global_max = max(max_1, max_2, ..., max_C) - global_sum = sum_i exp(max_i - global_max) * sum_i - output = (sum_i exp(max_i - global_max) * partial_output_i) / global_sum This can be done with an additional reduction kernel, or with atomic operations, or with a two-pass approach using global memory. Let me start with a simpler single-pass kernel (one block per KV head) and see how it performs. If it's not good enough, I'll add multi-chunk parallelism. Actually, you know what, let me just implement the kernel now. I'll start with a Triton version since it's faster to iterate on, then optimize to CUDA if needed. Wait, but Triton may struggle with the irregular memory access of paged attention. The `block_table` lookup means the memory access pattern is data-dependent. Triton's automatic pointer analysis might not handle this well. Let me go with CUDA C++ directly. Here's my plan: **Kernel: paged_attention_decode_kernel** Grid: (num_kv_heads, batch) — one block per KV head group per batch element Block dim: 256 threads Shared memory usage: - K_tile: (TILE_SIZE, HEAD_DIM) bf16 = TILE_SIZE * HEAD_DIM * 2 bytes - V_tile: (TILE_SIZE, HEAD_DIM) bf16 = TILE_SIZE * HEAD_DIM * 2 bytes With TILE_SIZE=16 (one page) and HEAD_DIM=128: 16*128*2*2 = 8192 bytes for K+V. Very manageable. Algorithm per block: ``` // Load Q heads for this KV head group // For each Q head h in [kv_head * group_size, (kv_head+1) * group_size): // q[h] = query[b, h, :] // Initialize online softmax state for each Q head: // max_val[h][d] or just max_val[h] (scalar max per head) // sum_exp[h] (scalar) // acc[h][d] (partial output, D elements) // For each page in the batch element's block table: // page_idx = block_table[b, page_num] // Load K_page = kv_cache[page_idx, :, kv_head, :D] // (page_size, D) // Load V_page = kv_cache[page_idx, :, kv_head, D:] // (page_size, D) // // For each Q head h: // scores_row = q[h] @ K_page^T // (D,) @ (D, page_size) -> (page_size,) // Update max_val[h], sum_exp[h], and rescale acc[h] // acc[h] += sum(softmax(scores_row) * V_page) // // After processing all pages (with proper sequence length truncation on last page): // output[b, h, :] = acc[h] / sum_exp[h] (already properly scaled from online softmax) ``` Wait, the online softmax algorithm needs to be done carefully. Let me write it out properly. For a single Q head with query q (D-dim vector): ``` m = -inf l = 0 o = zeros(D) For each tile of K_tile (T×D) and V_tile (T×D): s = q @ K_tile^T # (T,) — scores for this tile m_new = max(m, max(s)) # Rescale old accumulator o = o * exp(m - m_new) l = l * exp(m - m_new) # Add new tile p = exp(s - m_new) # (T,) l_new = l + sum(p) o = o + p @ V_tile # (D,) m = m_new l = l_new # Final: o = o / l ``` This is the standard FlashAttention online softmax. Each tile updates the running state. Now, for the actual implementation, since head_dim (128 or 64) is relatively small, I can keep the q vector, running max, sum, and output accumulator in registers for each Q head. With group_size up to 8 and D=128, that's 8 * 128 = 1024 floats for the accumulator alone, which is too many registers. So I need to use shared memory for some of this state, or process one Q head at a time within the block. Better approach: **Process one Q head at a time, but process the sequence once for all Q heads in the group.** Actually, the best strategy is: 1. Load K_tile and V_tile into shared memory (shared across all Q heads in the group) 2. Each warp (or set of warps) handles one Q head 3. Multiple Q heads in the group can be processed in parallel by different warps 4. Within a warp, use warp shuffles for the dot product reduction With group_size=4 (typical), and 8 warps per block (256 threads), we can have 2 warps per Q head or process 2 Q heads at a time in a loop. Wait, let me reconsider. With D=128 and a tile of T=16: - For one Q head, computing s = q @ K_tile^T is a (D,) @ (D, T) operation = T dot products of length D - Each dot product can be done by a warp (32 threads, each handling D/32 = 4 elements, with warp shuffle reduction) - Then T=16 dot products per tile, which is less than a warp's parallelism Actually, the most efficient approach is probably to have one warp per Q head, and each warp processes the tile: - Thread i in the warp handles elements i, i+32, i+64, i+96 of the D=128 dimension - Each thread computes partial dot products for all T=16 positions - Warp shuffle to reduce Let me think about this more practically. For D=128 and bf16: - Each thread loads D/32 = 4 elements of Q (as 2 bf16 pairs using uint2 loads from shared memory) - For each of the T=16 K positions, load 4 K elements, multiply-accumulate - Shuffle reduce across warp to get the 16 scores Then for V: - After softmax, each thread has 16 attention weights - Multiply each weight by corresponding V elements and accumulate - Shuffle reduce across warp to get D output elements This seems reasonable. Let me code it up. Actually, I realize I should also consider that for the case where D=64, D/32 = 2 elements per thread, which is less efficient. But we can handle that. Let me now write the actual kernel. I'll start with a reasonably optimized version. One more consideration: the Q @ K^T computation can be done at bf16 precision using tensor core operations. On SM120, we have `__hmma_mma_basic` or the newer wgmma instructions. But for a dot product of (1, D) @ (D, T), tensor cores aren't the best fit since they work on larger tiles (16×16×16 typically). For a single decode query, the Q @ K^T is essentially a GEMV (matrix-vector product), not a GEMM. Fused multiply-add in bf16 with warp shuffles is the right approach. OK, let me just write the kernel now. I'll start with CUDA C++ via load_inline. Here's my plan for the kernel: ```cuda // Each thread block handles one (batch, kv_head_group) // Grid: (num_kv_heads, batch) // Block: 256 threads (8 warps) // // The block processes all Q heads in its KV head group. // For group_size <= 8, each Q head gets at least one warp. // For group_size > 8, some Q heads share warps (processed sequentially). #define TILE_SIZE 16 // = page_size #define WARP_SIZE 32 __global__ void paged_attention_decode_kernel( const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ query, // (B, H, D) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ kv_cache, // (num_blocks, P, Hkv, 2*D) const int* __restrict__ block_table, // (B, max_blocks) const int* __restrict__ seq_lens, // (B,) __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ output, // (B, H, D) int B, int H, int Hkv, int D, int P, int max_blocks ) { int b = blockIdx.y; int kv_head = blockIdx.x; int group_size = H / Hkv; int seq_len = seq_lens[b]; if (seq_len <= 0) return; int num_pages = (seq_len + P - 1) / P; int last_page_tokens = seq_len - (num_pages - 1) * P; // If last_page_tokens == 0, it means seq_len is a multiple of P // Actually: num_pages = ceil(seq_len / P), last_page_tokens = seq_len % P, or P if 0 // Each warp handles one Q head (or cooperates on one) int warp_id = threadIdx.x / WARP_SIZE; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % WARP_SIZE; int num_warps = blockDim.x / WARP_SIZE; // Number of Q heads for this KV head: group_size // Assign warps to Q heads for (int q_offset = warp_id; q_offset < group_size; q_offset += num_warps) { int q_head = kv_head * group_size + q_offset; // Load Q for this head into registers // ... // Online softmax state float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float acc[MAX_D / WARP_SIZE]; // partial accumulators, reduced at the end // Process pages for (int page_idx = 0; page_idx < num_pages; page_idx++) { int block_idx = block_table[b * max_blocks + page_idx]; int tokens_this_page = (page_idx == num_pages - 1) ? last_page_tokens : P; // Load K and V for this page, this KV head // kv_cache[block_idx, :, kv_head, :] is (P, 2*D) // K is [:, :D], V is [:, D:] // For each position in this page, compute dot product with Q for (int t = 0; t < tokens_this_page; t++) { // ... } } // Write output // ... } } ``` Hmm, this is getting complex. Let me simplify and think about what the most efficient approach is. For a memory-bound kernel, the key metric is achieved memory bandwidth. Let me think about what limits bandwidth. The KV cache layout is: (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, head_dim*2) When loading K and V for a single KV head from a single page, the data is laid out as: - For position 0: kv_cache[block_idx, 0, kv_head, :] — 2*D = 256 bf16 values (512 bytes) - For position 1: kv_cache[block_idx, 1, kv_head, :] — another 512 bytes - etc. These are contiguous in memory! The stride between positions within a page is Hkv * 2*D = 8 * 256 = 2048 bf16 = 4096 bytes. Wait no, the layout is (page_size, num_kv_heads, head_dim*2), so: - kv_cache[block_idx, 0, kv_head, 0:2*D] is contiguous: 2*D bf16 values = 512 bytes - kv_cache[block_idx, 1, kv_head, 0:2*D] is also contiguous, starting 2*D*Hkv = 512*8 = 4096 bytes later So loading one position's K and V for one KV head is a contiguous 512-byte load — that's 32 x 128-bit loads. Very efficient! The access pattern for loading an entire page's K and V for one KV head: - Load 16 × 256 bf16 = 4096 bf16 = 8192 bytes - But these 16 loads are strided by 4096 bytes (the space for other KV heads within the page) This stride means we can't do a single coalesced load for the whole page — but we can do coalesced loads per position. Each position's K+V is contiguous. For better efficiency, we could transpose the loading: load position 0 for all 16 positions... no, that doesn't help because each position is already contiguous. Actually, the most efficient approach is to load K and V for all positions in a tile together, vectorizing across the head_dim. Since head_dim is 128 or 64, and we load with uint4 (16 bytes = 8 bf16 values), each position requires D/8 uint4 loads for K and D/8 for V. For D=128: 128/8 = 16 uint4 loads for K, 16 for V = 32 loads per position × 16 positions = 512 uint4 loads per page per KV head. That's 512 * 16 = 8192 bytes per page per KV head — exactly what we computed. With 512 transactions of 16 bytes each: these are fully coalesced if all threads in a warp participate. With 32 threads, each thread does 512/32 = 16 uint4 loads. If the threads load different positions/offsets, we need to be careful about coalescing. Let me now think about the threading strategy more carefully. **Thread mapping for a single (batch, kv_head) block:** Option A: One warp per Q head, all warps cooperate on loading KV tiles - Warp 0 loads K and V tiles into shared memory - Each warp then computes attention for its Q head using the shared memory Option B: Threads cooperate across Q heads to load KV tiles - All threads load K and V into shared memory - Then each warp processes a different Q head Option A is cleaner. Let's say we have 256 threads (8 warps): - For loading: all 8 warps cooperate. Each warp loads part of the K and V tiles. - For compute: each warp handles one Q head. With group_size=4 (typical), 4 warps are active, 4 idle. With group_size=8, all 8 warps active. Actually, let me make it simpler. I'll use one warp per Q head, and have the warps independently load the K and V they need from global memory (no shared memory caching of KV). This avoids shared memory bank conflicts and synchronization, at the cost of redundant loads. Since the kernel is memory-bound anyway, the redundant loads might hurt. Better: use shared memory. Load each page's K and V into shared memory once, then all warps use it. This is especially beneficial for GQA where group_size > 1. Shared memory layout for one page tile: - K_tile: (P, D) = (16, 128) bf16 = 4096 bytes - V_tile: (P, D) = (16, 128) bf16 = 4096 bytes Total: 8192 bytes — fits easily in 48KB shared memory. **Revised plan:** For each (batch, kv_head) block: 1. Initialize per-Q-head state in registers (one Q head per warp) 2. For each page: a. All threads load K_tile and V_tile into shared memory b. __syncthreads() c. Each warp computes partial attention for its Q head using shared memory K_tile and V_tile d. Update online softmax state e. __syncthreads() (for next page load) 3. Write final output For loading K and V into shared memory from a page: - kv_cache[block_idx, :, kv_head, 0:D] is K: shape (P, D) with stride Hkv*2*D between positions - Each thread loads a few elements from global to shared memory For P=16 and D=128: - 16 positions × 128 elements = 2048 bf16 elements for K - With 256 threads: each thread loads 2048/256 = 8 bf16 elements for K, 8 for V - Using uint4 (8 bf16): each thread does 1 uint4 load for K and 1 for V - Perfect! With D=128 and 8 bf16 per load, each position needs 16 loads. With 256 threads, 16 positions × 16 loads = 256 loads. Each thread does one load. Perfect! Let me write the initial solution. I'll create a CUDA kernel using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. Let me design the kernel carefully. The kernel processes pages of the KV cache for a specific (batch_element, kv_head) pair. Since this is GQA, each kv_head serves group_size = num_heads / num_kv_heads query heads. Algorithm for one (batch, kv_head): 1. For each query head in the group, initialize online softmax state (m=-inf, l=0, acc=0) 2. For each page assigned to this batch element: a. Load K and V for this page, this kv_head into shared memory b. For each query head, compute partial attention: - scores = q @ K^T (shape: page_size, but truncated on last page) - m_new = max(m, max(scores)) - rescale acc and l by exp(m - m_new) - p = exp(scores - m_new) - l += sum(p) - acc += p @ V 3. Write acc / l to output Within a block, I'll use warps to parallelize over query heads. With 8 warps (256 threads) and group_size up to 8, each warp can handle one query head. For each warp computing one query head's attention on a tile: - Thread i handles elements i, i+32, i+64, i+96 of D (for D=128, 4 elements per thread) - Compute partial dot product for each position in the tile - Warp shuffle to sum across threads - Apply softmax and accumulate V Let me now code this up. Actually, I need to be very careful about the shared memory layout and the loading pattern. Let me define: Shared memory for one page: - K_tile: (PAGE_SIZE, HEAD_DIM) bf16 - V_tile: (PAGE_SIZE, HEAD_DIM) bf16 Where PAGE_SIZE=16 and HEAD_DIM is 128 or 64. For D=128: K_tile is 16*128 = 2048 bf16 = 4096 bytes. Same for V_tile. Total 8192 bytes. For D=64: K_tile is 16*64 = 1024 bf16 = 2048 bytes. Total 4096 bytes. With 256 threads: - D=128: Each thread loads 2048/256 = 8 bf16 elements for K and 8 for V = 16 bf16 total. That's 2 uint4 loads per thread. - D=64: Each thread loads 1024/256 = 4 bf16 for K and 4 for V = 8 bf16 total. 1 uint4 load per thread. For the loading pattern, given a page at `kv_cache[page_idx, :, kv_head, :]`: - Position pos (0..PAGE_SIZE-1): K at offset pos * (Hkv * 2*D) + kv_head * (2*D), V at offset + D - Each thread needs to load specific elements. Actually, the memory layout is more complex. The kv_cache tensor has shape (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, 2*head_dim). So: - kv_cache[page_idx, pos, kv_head, d] accesses element d of the packed [K|V] at position pos, KV head kv_head, in block page_idx. In row-major (C) order: - Stride of page_idx dim: page_size * num_kv_heads * 2*head_dim - Stride of pos dim: num_kv_heads * 2*head_dim - Stride of kv_head dim: 2*head_dim So for a given page_idx, the K for KV head kv_head is: - kv_cache[page_idx, 0, kv_head, 0:D] = starting at page_idx * P * Hkv * 2*D + 0 * Hkv * 2*D + kv_head * 2*D, length D - kv_cache[page_idx, 1, kv_head, 0:D] = starting at page_idx * P * Hkv * 2*D + 1 * Hkv * 2*D + kv_head * 2*D, length D - etc. So K[pos, :] is D contiguous elements, and V[pos, :] is the D elements at offset D from the K start. For loading into shared memory, each thread loads a chunk of (pos, element_range). Let me use a flat mapping: All threads cooperatively load K_tile[pos][d] for all pos in 0..PAGE_SIZE-1 and all d in 0..D-1, then V_tile similarly. With 256 threads and PAGE_SIZE*D = 16*128 = 2048 elements of K: - Each thread loads 2048/256 = 8 elements of K (and similarly for V) - Use uint4 for 8 bf16 at a time Map thread `tid` to (pos, d_group) where pos = tid / (D/8) and d_group = tid % (D/8): - Wait, 16 * (128/8) = 16 * 16 = 256. So tid uniquely maps to (pos, d_group) where d_group is which group of 8 bf16 elements. So thread `tid` loads K at position pos=tid/16, d_group=tid%16: `kv_cache[page_idx, pos, kv_head, d_group*8 : (d_group+1)*8]` — 8 contiguous bf16 values. And V similarly: `kv_cache[page_idx, pos, kv_head, D + d_group*8 : D + (d_group+1)*8]` This is perfect for D=128! Each thread does exactly one uint4 load for K and one for V. For D=64: 16 * (64/8) = 16 * 8 = 128 threads needed. We can use tid < 128 for loading, and threads 128-255 idle during load. OK, this is solid. Let me now write the full kernel. Now, for the warp-level computation: Each warp (32 threads) handles one query head. The query head `q_head` has: - Q vector: query[b, q_head, :] of shape (D,) — we load this into registers at the start For each tile (page) of K and V in shared memory: - K_tile[t, d] for t in 0..tokens-1, d in 0..D-1 - V_tile[t, d] for t in 0..tokens-1, d in 0..D-1 Compute scores[t] = sum_d Q[d] * K_tile[t, d]: - Thread lane `i` (0..31) handles elements d = i, i+32, i+64, i+96 of D - For each position t: thread i computes partial = Q[i] * K_tile[t, i] + Q[i+32] * K_tile[t, i+32] + ... - Then warp shuffle to sum all 32 partials into a single score per position t For D=128: - Each thread handles 4 elements: d = lane, lane+32, lane+64, lane+96 - Load Q values once, then for each t, load 4 K values from shared memory For D=64: - Each thread handles 2 elements: d = lane, lane+32 - Same pattern but fewer iterations After computing scores for the tile, apply online softmax update: ``` scores_f[t] = float(scores[t]) * scale m_new = max(m, max(scores_f)) // Rescale for d in thread's elements: acc[d] *= exp(m - m_new) acc[d+32] *= exp(m - m_new) ... l *= exp(m - m_new) // Compute new probs and accumulate for t in 0..tokens-1: p = exp(scores_f[t] - m_new) / (no, we don't normalize yet) // Actually: p = exp(scores_f[t] - m_new), and we accumulate l += p for d in thread's elements: acc[d] += p * V_tile[t, d] m = m_new ``` Wait, I should be more careful. The online softmax correctly: ``` m_new = max(m_old, max(scores)) // rescale old accumulator acc *= exp(m_old - m_new) l *= exp(m_old - m_new) // add new contributions p = exp(scores - m_new) l += sum(p) acc += p @ V m = m_new ``` At the end (after all tiles): ``` output = acc / l ``` And I need to handle the scale factor: scores = (Q @ K^T) * scale. So scores_f[t] = dot(Q, K[t]) * scale. Let me code this up now. I'll keep it clean and use float32 for all accumulations. For the shared memory layout, let me use: - `extern __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 smem[];` with K_tile at offset 0 and V_tile at offset PAGE_SIZE * HEAD_DIM. Actually, I'll use a fixed-size shared memory array. Let me define max D = 128. ```cuda constexpr int MAX_D = 128; constexpr int PAGE_SIZE = 16; __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 K_smem[PAGE_SIZE * MAX_D]; __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 V_smem[PAGE_SIZE * MAX_D]; ``` Wait, I can't use `extern __shared__` with templates easily without knowing D at compile time. But since D is passed as a runtime argument (or template), I should handle this. Let me use templates for D to get efficient code: ```cuda template<int D> __global__ void paged_attention_decode_kernel(...) { constexpr int PAGE_SIZE = 16; __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 K_smem[PAGE_SIZE * D]; __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 V_smem[PAGE_SIZE * D]; // ... } ``` But then I need to instantiate for D=128 and D=64. And also handle different group_sizes. This gets complex. Let me just use a single kernel with runtime D and use `extern __shared__` for the shared memory. I'll compute the shared memory size as `2 * PAGE_SIZE * D * sizeof(__nv_bfloat16)`. Actually, there's a simpler approach: just use the maximum D (128) for shared memory allocation and pad: ```cuda __global__ void paged_attention_decode_kernel( ... int D ) { constexpr int PAGE_SIZE = 16; constexpr int MAX_D = 128; __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 K_smem[PAGE_SIZE * MAX_D]; __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 V_smem[PAGE_SIZE * MAX_D]; // Use D for loop bounds } ``` This wastes some shared memory for D=64 (allocates 4096 bytes but uses only 2048), but it's fine. Hmm, but 2 * 16 * 128 * 2 = 8192 bytes for K+V shared memory. That's well within the 49KB limit. OK let me finalize and write the kernel. I need to handle: 1. Multiple query heads in the group (up to 8) 2. Pages of K/V cache 3. Non-power-of-2 sequence lengths (truncated last page) 4. Online softmax Let me write it now. I'll use one warp per query head for the computation, and all threads for loading K/V. Let me also handle the case where group_size > NUM_WARPS (e.g., group_size=8 with 4 warps). Wait, I just want to double-check: for group_size=8 (shape 3 with H=64, Hkv=8), each warp handles one Q head, and we have exactly 8 warps for 8 Q heads. Let me code: ```cuda constexpr int NUM_WARPS = 8; constexpr int WARP_SIZE = 32; constexpr int PAGE_SIZE = 16; constexpr int MAX_D = 128; __global__ void paged_attention_decode_kernel( const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ query, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ kv_cache, const int* __restrict__ block_table, const int* __restrict__ seq_lens, __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int Hkv, int D, int max_blocks ) { int b = blockIdx.y; int kv_head = blockIdx.x; int group_size = H / Hkv; int seq_len = seq_lens[b]; if (seq_len <= 0) return; int num_pages = (seq_len + PAGE_SIZE - 1) / PAGE_SIZE; __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 K_smem[PAGE_SIZE * MAX_D]; __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 V_smem[PAGE_SIZE * MAX_D]; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / WARP_SIZE; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % WARP_SIZE; // Each warp handles one Q head (or multiple if group_size > NUM_WARPS) // But group_size <= NUM_WARPS since max is 8 int q_head_in_group = warp_id; if (q_head_in_group >= group_size) return; // idle warp int q_head = kv_head * group_size + q_head_in_group; // Load Q for this head into registers float q_reg[MAX_D / WARP_SIZE]; // per-thread portion of Q #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D / WARP_SIZE; i++) { int d = lane_id + i * WARP_SIZE; q_reg[i] = __bfloat162float(query[b * H * D + q_head * D + d]); } // Online softmax state float m = -1e30f; float l = 0.0f; float acc[MAX_D / WARP_SIZE] = {0}; float scale = rsqrtf(float(D)); // Process pages for (int page_idx = 0; page_idx < num_pages; page_idx++) { int block_idx = block_table[b * max_blocks + page_idx]; int tokens_this_page = (page_idx == num_pages - 1 && seq_len % PAGE_SIZE != 0) ? seq_len % PAGE_SIZE : PAGE_SIZE; // Cooperative load of K and V into shared memory // All threads (including idle warps) participate in loading int tid = threadIdx.x; int total_elems_per_tile = PAGE_SIZE * D; int num_threads = blockDim.x; // Load K for (int idx = tid; idx < total_elems_per_tile; idx += num_threads) { int pos = idx / D; int d = idx % D; K_smem[idx] = kv_cache[block_idx * (PAGE_SIZE * Hkv * 2 * D) + pos * (Hkv * 2 * D) + kv_head * (2 * D) + d]; } // Load V for (int idx = tid; idx < total_elems_per_tile; idx += num_threads) { int pos = idx / D; int d = idx % D; V_smem[idx] = kv_cache[block_idx * (PAGE_SIZE * Hkv * 2 * D) + pos * (Hkv * 2 * D) + kv_head * (2 * D) + D + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Only the warp handling this Q head does the computation if (q_head_in_group < group_size) { // Compute scores for all positions in this tile float scores[PAGE_SIZE]; float m_local = m; float max_score = m; // start with current max #pragma unroll for (int t = 0; t < tokens_this_page; t++) { // Dot product: sum over d of Q[d] * K[t, d] float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D / WARP_SIZE; i++) { int d = lane_id + i * WARP_SIZE; dot += q_reg[i] * __bfloat162float(K_smem[t * D + d]); } // Warp reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = WARP_SIZE / 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { dot += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, dot, offset); } scores[t] = dot * scale; if (scores[t] > max_score) max_score = scores[t]; } float m_new = max_score; float rescale = expf(m - m_new); // Rescale accumulator #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D / WARP_SIZE; i++) { acc[i] *= rescale; } l *= rescale; // Add new contributions float l_local = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int t = 0; t < tokens_this_page; t++) { float p = expf(scores[t] - m_new); l_local += p; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D / WARP_SIZE; i++) { int d = lane_id + i * WARP_SIZE; acc[i] += p * __bfloat162float(V_smem[t * D + d]); } } // Warp reduce l_local #pragma unroll for (int offset = WARP_SIZE / 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { l_local += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, l_local, offset); } l += l_local; m = m_new; } __syncthreads(); } // Write output if (q_head_in_group < group_size) { float inv_l = 1.0f / l; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D / WARP_SIZE; i++) { int d = lane_id + i * WARP_SIZE; output[b * H * D + q_head * D + d] = __float2bfloat16(acc[i] * inv_l); } } } ``` Wait, I have a bug: idle warps should participate in loading K/V but then skip the computation. Let me restructure: all threads load K/V (since we need those warps for loading to cover all elements), but only the assigned warp does the computation. Actually, looking at my code above, the load loops use `tid` which is `threadIdx.x`, so ALL threads participate regardless of warp assignment. That's correct. The computation is guarded by `if (q_head_in_group < group_size)`. But wait, there's a subtle issue. I defined `q_head_in_group = warp_id` and then `if (q_head_in_group >= group_size) return` at the beginning. This means idle warps would return immediately and not participate in the K/V loading. Let me fix this by removing the early return and using the guard only around computation. Let me restructure the kernel more carefully. Actually, I realize there's another issue: the K/V loading loops load elements one bf16 at a time. This is inefficient. I should use vectorized loads (uint4 = 8 bf16 values). Let me use the proper approach. Also, the memory offsets are wrong. The kv_cache tensor is a torch tensor with shape (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, 2*head_dim). In memory, it's row-major: - Offset of element [b, p, h, d] = b * (P * Hkv * 2*D) + p * (Hkv * 2*D) + h * (2*D) + d But the `kv_cache` pointer from PyTorch is already a flat pointer to the data, so these offsets are correct for direct pointer arithmetic. However, I can also just use the pointer with appropriate indexing. Wait, but actually, PyTorch tensors may have strides that differ from the shape. The `data_ptr()` returns a pointer to the first element, and all indexing should use strides. However, for `load_inline`, the tensors should be contiguous (they're created with `torch.randn`), and `get_inputs` ensures contiguity. Let me use simpler indexing. I'll define a helper macro or compute base offsets. Actually, let me reconsider my approach. Instead of manual pointer arithmetic with potential stride issues, let me pass the stride values as kernel arguments or compute them. The kv_cache strides are: - stride_0 (block): P * Hkv * 2 * D - stride_1 (position): Hkv * 2 * D - stride_2 (kv_head): 2 * D - stride_3 (element): 1 Since we're working with a contiguous tensor from `get_inputs()`, these are the actual strides. But I should be safe and compute the strides. OK, let me pass more parameters. Actually, for simplicity and safety, let me just pass the full stride information. Hmm, let me look at how vLLM does it. They typically pass the data pointer and strides. Let me keep it simple and assume contiguous layout since `get_inputs()` generates contiguous tensors. Let me also think about the load pattern more carefully. With PAGE_SIZE=16 and D=128: - K for one page, one KV head: 16 * 128 = 2048 bf16 values - With 256 threads: each loads 8 values. Using uint4 (8 bf16), each thread does 1 load. The K data for position pos is at: `base = block_idx * (P * Hkv * 2 * D) + pos * (Hkv * 2 * D) + kv_head * (2 * D)` and the D elements are contiguous. Thread `tid` maps to: - pos = tid / (D/8) = tid / 16 (for D=128) - chunk = tid % 16 Loads K at base + chunk*8 (8 bf16 values = 1 uint4) Similarly for V at base + D + chunk*8. Wait, but for D=64, D/8 = 8, so pos = tid / 8 and chunk = tid % 8. With 256 threads, tid 0..127 covers all positions, and threads 128..255 would map to pos >= 16 (invalid). I need to handle this. Let me use a loop-based approach where each thread loads multiple elements: ```cuda int total_k_elems = PAGE_SIZE * D; // 16*128=2048 or 16*64=1024 int elems_per_thread = (total_k_elems + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int i = 0; i < elems_per_thread; i++) { int idx = threadIdx.x + i * blockDim.x; if (idx < total_k_elems) { int pos = idx / D; int d = idx % D; // Load K_smem[pos * D + d] } } ``` This handles both D=128 and D=64 without special casing. But it loads single bf16 values. For better bandwidth, I should use vectorized loads. Actually, for the cooperative load, I should pack 8 bf16 values (uint4) per load. Let me restructure: ```cuda // Each thread loads chunks of 8 bf16 using uint4 int elems_per_chunk = 8; // sizeof(uint4) / sizeof(__nv_bfloat16) = 8 int total_chunks_K = (PAGE_SIZE * D) / elems_per_chunk; // 2048/8=256 or 1024/8=128 for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < total_chunks_K; i += blockDim.x) { int pos = i / (D / elems_per_chunk); int chunk = i % (D / elems_per_chunk); int global_d = chunk * elems_per_chunk; // Load 8 bf16 values } ``` This is cleaner. With D=128: total_chunks_K = 256, each of 256 threads loads 1 chunk. With D=64: total_chunks_K = 128, threads 0..127 load 1 chunk each, threads 128..255 are idle. For the uint4 load, I need to load from global memory. Let me cast the pointer appropriately. Actually, for loading bf16 values one at a time, the compiler might coalesce them anyway. Using `__nv_bfloat16` loads should result in 128-bit transactions when adjacent threads access adjacent addresses. But for safety, let me use uint4 explicitly. Hmm, let me simplify. For the initial implementation, let me just do byte-level loads or single bf16 loads and optimize later. The correctness comes first. Actually, let me use a different approach. Each thread handles loading specific positions. Since D=128, 8 bf16 per uint4: - 128 / 8 = 16 uint4 loads per position - 16 positions: 256 unique loads - 256 threads: perfect 1:1 mapping I'll use `reinterpret_cast<const uint4*>` for the loads. +"""Paged attention decode kernel for RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell).
+
+CUDA C++ kernel with online softmax, shared-memory tiling, and vectorized loads.
+"""
+
+import math
+from typing import List
+
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+
+OP_TYPE = "attention"
+SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
+HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
+
+BATCH = 8
+NUM_HEADS = 32
+NUM_KV_HEADS = 8
+HEAD_DIM = 128
+SEQ_LEN = 1024
+PAGE_SIZE = 16
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# CUDA kernel
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+_cuda_kernel_src = r"""
+#include <cuda_fp16.h>
+#include <cuda_bf16.h>
+
+constexpr int PAGE_SIZE = 16;
+constexpr int MAX_D = 128;
+constexpr int WARP_SIZE = 32;
+constexpr int MAX_GROUP_SIZE = 8;  // max num_heads/num_kv_heads across shapes
+
+extern "C" __global__ void paged_attention_decode_kernel(
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ query,       // (B, H, D)
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ kv_cache,    // (num_blocks, P, Hkv, 2*D)
+    const int* __restrict__ block_table,           // (B, max_blocks)
+    const int* __restrict__ seq_lens,              // (B,)
+    __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ output,            // (B, H, D)
+    int B,
+    int H,
+    int Hkv,
+    int D,
+    int max_blocks,
+    int kv_cache_stride_block,   // stride of block dim in bf16 elements
+    int kv_cache_stride_pos,     // stride of position dim
+    int kv_cache_stride_head     // stride of kv_head dim
+) {
+    int b = blockIdx.y;
+    int kv_head = blockIdx.x;
+    int group_size = H / Hkv;
+    int seq_len = seq_lens[b];
+    if (seq_len <= 0) return;
+
+    int num_pages = (seq_len + PAGE_SIZE - 1) / PAGE_SIZE;
+
+    __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 K_smem[PAGE_SIZE * MAX_D];
+    __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 V_smem[PAGE_SIZE * MAX_D];
+
+    int warp_id = threadIdx.x / WARP_SIZE;
+    int lane_id = threadIdx.x % WARP_SIZE;
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+    int num_threads = blockDim.x;
+
+    // Map each warp to a Q head in the group.
+    // With up to 8 warps (256 threads) and max group_size=8, each Q head
+    // gets one dedicated warp.  If group_size < 8, extra warps idle.
+    int q_head_in_group = warp_id;
+    bool active = (q_head_in_group < group_size);
+    int q_head = kv_head * group_size + q_head_in_group;
+
+    // --- Pre-load Q for this warp's head into registers ---
+    float q_reg[MAX_D / WARP_SIZE];
+    if (active) {
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int i = 0; i < D / WARP_SIZE; i++) {
+            int d = lane_id + i * WARP_SIZE;
+            if (d < D) {
+                q_reg[i] = __bfloat162float(query[b * H * D + q_head * D + d]);
+            } else {
+                q_reg[i] = 0.0f;
+            }
+        }
+    }
+
+    // --- Online-softmax state (fp32, per thread's stripe of D) ---
+    float m_global = -1e30f;
+    float l_global = 0.0f;
+    float acc[MAX_D / WARP_SIZE];
+    if (active) {
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int i = 0; i < D / WARP_SIZE; i++) {
+            acc[i] = 0.0f;
+        }
+    }
+
+    float scale = rsqrtf(static_cast<float>(D));
+
+    // --- Page loop ---
+    for (int page_idx = 0; page_idx < num_pages; page_idx++) {
+        int block_idx = block_table[b * max_blocks + page_idx];
+        int tokens_this_page;
+        if (page_idx == num_pages - 1) {
+            int rem = seq_len % PAGE_SIZE;
+            tokens_this_page = (rem == 0) ? PAGE_SIZE : rem;
+        } else {
+            tokens_this_page = PAGE_SIZE;
+        }
+
+        // ----- Cooperative load of K and V into shared memory -----
+        // All threads (including idle warps) participate so we fill smem
+        // quickly with coalesced accesses.
+        int total_k_elems = PAGE_SIZE * D;
+        int elems_per_thread = (total_k_elems + num_threads - 1) / num_threads;
+
+        // Load K
+        for (int i = 0; i < elems_per_thread; i++) {
+            int idx = tid + i * num_threads;
+            if (idx < total_k_elems) {
+                int pos = idx / D;
+                int d   = idx % D;
+                int gmem_offset = block_idx * kv_cache_stride_block
+                                + pos * kv_cache_stride_pos
+                                + kv_head * kv_cache_stride_head
+                                + d;
+                K_smem[idx] = kv_cache[gmem_offset];
+            }
+        }
+
+        // Load V
+        for (int i = 0; i < elems_per_thread; i++) {
+            int idx = tid + i * num_threads;
+            if (idx < total_k_elems) {
+                int pos = idx / D;
+                int d   = idx % D;
+                int gmem_offset = block_idx * kv_cache_stride_block
+                                + pos * kv_cache_stride_pos
+                                + kv_head * kv_cache_stride_head
+                                + D + d;
+                V_smem[idx] = kv_cache[gmem_offset];
+            }
+        }
+
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        // ----- Per-warp attention computation -----
+        if (active) {
+            // Compute scores[t] = dot(Q, K[t]) * scale for every valid
+            // position in this tile, using warp shuffles for the reduction.
+            float scores[PAGE_SIZE];
+
+            float m_local = m_global;
+            float max_score = m_global;
+
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int t = 0; t < PAGE_SIZE; t++) {
+                float dot = 0.0f;
+                if (t < tokens_this_page) {
+                    #pragma unroll
+                    for (int i = 0; i < D / WARP_SIZE; i++) {
+                        int d = lane_id + i * WARP_SIZE;
+                        if (d < D) {
+                            dot += q_reg[i] * __bfloat162float(K_smem[t * D + d]);
+                        }
+                    }
+                }
+                // Full-warp reduction for

... (6117 more chars)

Triton doesn't support `__setitem__` for tensor assignment. I need to use a different approach. Let me restructure the kernel to avoid setitem. Instead of accumulating Q into a 2D tensor, I'll compute per-query-head attention separately by looping over g in the outer part, or use a different tensor construction approach. Actually, looking at this more carefully, I think the fundamental issue is that Triton's programming model works differently from what I wrote. Let me redesign the kernel. For Triton, the typical approach for batched operations is to use a 2D program grid. In this case, each program handles one (batch, kv_head) pair, and within that, I loop over the group of Q heads. But the issue is how to store the per-Q-head state. I can't do `q[g, :] = ...` because setitem isn't supported. Instead, I need to compute all Q heads' dot products in a single operation, or process one Q head at a time. Let me redesign: Option 1: Compute QK^T for all group Q heads at once by loading Q as a (group_size, D) matrix. But I need to construct this matrix first. Actually, in Triton, I can load all Q rows using a single load with broadcasting: ```python q_offs = pid_b * stride_q_b + kv_head * group_size * stride_q_h + tl.arange(0, group_size)[:, None] * stride_q_h + offs_d[None, :] ``` But wait, this loads (group_size, BLOCK_D) directly. Let me try this. The issue is the stride: `q_head_idx * stride_q_h` where `q_head_idx = kv_head * group_size + g`. So offset would be: - `pid_b * stride_q_b + (kv_head * group_size) * stride_q_h + g * stride_q_h + offs_d` Can I express this as `pid_b * stride_q_b + q_base_offset + g[:, None] * stride_q_h + offs_d[None, :]`? Let me restructure the entire kernel to use this pattern. Actually, let me step back and think about the simplest correct approach. The most straightforward Triton kernel for this: - Grid: (B, Hkv, num_pages_per_seq) — but num_pages varies, so that's awkward - Or: Grid: (B, Hkv) — each program handles the entire sequence for one (batch, kv_head) Within each program: - Loop over pages - For each page, load K and V tiles - For each Q head in the group, compute QK^T and update online softmax Since I can't store all Q heads' state in a 2D tensor using setitem, I have two options: 1. Process Q heads sequentially (loop over g), keeping per-head state in scalars/small arrays 2. Keep per-head state in separate 1D arrays and iterate over heads in the inner loop Option 2 is better for parallelism. Let me use a pattern where I process Q heads sequentially but keep the page loop outer (load K/V once per page, then process all Q heads): ``` for page: load K_tile, V_tile for g in range(group_size): scores = dot(Q[g], K_tile) * scale update online softmax for head g accumulate V for head g ``` With this approach, per-head state can be stored as separate variables or arrays. Actually wait, in Triton, can I have an array of per-Q-head state? Like `m = tl.zeros([group_size], dtype=tl.float32)` and then index into it with `m[g]`? Let me check... `tl.zeros([group_size], ...)` creates a 1D tensor. Can I do `m[g] = ...`? I think Triton supports scalar indexing (getting/setting individual elements), but not slice assignment. Let me try using `tl.where` and `tl.broadcast_to` patterns, or just use separate scalar variables and loop. Actually, for group_size up to 8, I can just unroll by hand or use separate scalar variables and conditionals. But that's ugly. Let me try a different approach: use a 3D program grid (B, Hkv, group_inner_g) where each program handles exactly ONE query head. This avoids the per-head state accumulation issue entirely. Grid: (B, Hkv, group_size) but Triton grids are 1D or 2D. I can flatten: - Grid: (B * Hkv, group_size) or equivalently (B, Hkv * group_size) but that's (B, H). Wait, if grid is (B, H), each program handles one query head. Then: - Program (b, h) handles query head h for batch element b - kv_head = h // group_size - The K/V for this kv_head is loaded by all programs that share the same kv_head This means multiple programs load the same K/V data from global memory. But with L2 caching, this can be efficient. The per-program state is just for ONE query head, so it's simple: ``` m = -inf l = 0 acc = zeros(BLOCK_D) for each page: load K_tile, V_tile scores = dot(q[h], K_tile) * scale update m, l, acc output[h] = acc / l ``` This is much cleaner! Let me implement this. But wait, the key issue: loading Q. Each program needs query[pid_b, pid_h, :]. - q_offs = pid_b * stride_q_b + pid_h * stride_q_h + offs_d - q = tl.load(query_ptr + q_offs, mask=offs_d < D) Then for each page, load K and V for the corresponding KV head, and compute attention. Let me rewrite the kernel with this approach. But there's a concern: if I use grid (B, H), that's up to 32*32 = 1024 programs for shape 2, or 4*64 = 256 for shape 3. Each program loads its own K and V pages. This means the same K and V pages are loaded group_size times (once per Q head in the group). For group_size=8, that's 8x redundant loads. However, with the L2 cache on Blackwell being quite large, the redundant loads should mostly hit L2 and not DRAM. This is a common trade-off in attention kernels. Alternatively, I can use a smaller grid (B, Hkv) and process all group Q heads within each program. But I need to handle the setitem limitation. Let me try the per-Q-head approach first (grid=(B, H)) since it's simpler and should still be fast with L2 caching. Actually, I just had another idea. What if I store per-Q-head state using multiple 1D tensors and manually index them? Something like: ```python m = tl.zeros([group_size], dtype=tl.float32) # ... update m[g] = new_val # Instead of m[g] = ..., use: m = tl.where(tl.arange(0, group_size) == g, new_val, m) ``` Hmm, that's awkward for updating individual elements. But for max, I can compute the new max per head and use where: ```python scores_g = ... # per-head scores for this page m_new_per_head = tl.max(scores_g, axis=1) # (group_size,) m = tl.maximum(m, m_new_per_head) ``` That works if I can compute all heads' scores at once. The challenge is loading all Q heads' data at once. Let me think about loading all Q heads' data into a (group_size, BLOCK_D) tensor without using setitem. I can construct the offsets as a 2D grid: ```python offs_g = tl.arange(0, group_size) # (group_size,) q_offs_2d = pid_b * stride_q_b + (kv_head * group_size + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_q_h + offs_d[None, :] # shape: (group_size, BLOCK_D) q = tl.load(query_ptr + q_offs_2d, mask=offs_d[None, :] < D, other=0.0) ``` This loads all Q heads in the group in one shot! No setitem needed. Let me use this approach. Then the per-head state needs to be maintained. Since Triton is SSA (static single assignment), I need to use `tl.where` or accumulator patterns. Actually, for the online softmax update, I can do it all vectorized: ```python # m: (group_size,), l: (group_size,), acc: (group_size, BLOCK_D) # scores: (group_size, BLOCK_L) — computed as Q @ K^T scores = tl.dot(q.to(tl.float32), tl.trans(k_tile.to(tl.float32))) * scale scores = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], scores, float("-inf")) m_new = tl.maximum(m, tl.max(scores, axis=1)) # (group_size,) rescale = tl.exp(m - m_new) # (group_size,) acc = acc * rescale[:, None] # broadcast l = l * rescale p = tl.exp(scores - m_new[:, None]) # (group_size, BLOCK_L) p = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], p, 0.0) l = l + tl.sum(p, axis=1) acc = acc + tl.dot(p, v_tile.to(tl.float32)) m = m_new ``` This is all vectorized! Much better. The key was loading Q as a 2D tensor using 2D offset arrays. Let me rewrite the kernel now. But wait, there's another issue: Triton's `tl.dot` expects specific input shapes. The first argument to `tl.dot` should be (M, K) and the second (K, N), producing (M, N). For `scores = tl.dot(q_f32, tl.trans(k_f32))`: - q_f32: (group_size, BLOCK_D) - k_f32: (BLOCK_L, BLOCK_D) - k_f32^T: (BLOCK_D, BLOCK_L) - Result: (group_size, BLOCK_L) ✓ For `acc = acc + tl.dot(p, v_f32)`: - p: (group_size, BLOCK_L) - v_f32: (BLOCK_L, BLOCK_D) - Result: (group_size, BLOCK_D) ✓ Looks good! The constexpr requirement is for the BLOCK sizes. `group_size` can be a runtime value as long as the matrix dimensions are concrete. But the autotuner keys on `["D", "group_size"]` — so different (D, group_size) combos get different configs. For D=64 with group_size=4, one set; for D=128 with group_size=8, another. stdout (532 chars)
@contextmanager
def numeric_stress_context(
    ref_model: torch.nn.Module,
    sol_model: torch.nn.Module,
    inputs: Sequence[object],
    case: NumericStressCase,
) -> Iterator[list[object]]:
    backups = _scale_states([ref_model, sol_model], case.state_scales)
    try:
        yield _scale_inputs(inputs, case.input_scales)
    finally:
        if torch.cuda.is_available():
            torch.cuda.synchronize()
        with torch.no_grad():
            for tensor, original in backups:
                tensor.copy_(original)
assistant

tool
stdout (486 chars)
def _scale_inputs(inputs: Sequence[object], scales: Mapping[int, float]) -> list[object]:
    out = list(inputs)
    for idx, scale in scales.items():
        if idx >= len(out):
            raise IndexError(f"input scale index {idx} out of range for {len(out)} inputs")
        value = out[idx]
        if not isinstance(value, torch.Tensor):
            raise TypeError(f"input {idx} is not a tensor and cannot be scaled")
        out[idx] = _scale_tensor(value, scale)
    return out

stdout (277 chars)
def _scale_tensor(tensor: torch.Tensor, scale: float) -> torch.Tensor:
    if not torch.is_floating_point(tensor):
        raise TypeError(f"cannot scale non-floating tensor dtype={tensor.dtype}")
    return (tensor.float() * scale).to(dtype=tensor.dtype, device=tensor.device)

import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '/workspace')
import torch
import reference
import triton
import triton.language as tl
from src.eval.numeric_stress import numeric_stress_cases, numeric_stress_context, tolerance_for_case
from src.eval.correctness import check_correctness
import shapes, yaml
from pathlib import Path

meta = yaml.safe_load(Path('problem.yaml').read_text())
device = torch.device('cuda:0')

shape = shapes.SHAPES[0]
reference.BATCH = shape['batch']
reference.NUM_HEADS = shape['num_heads']
reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape['num_kv_heads']
reference.HEAD_DIM = shape['head_dim']
reference.SEQ_LEN = shape['seq_len']
reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape['page_size']

# Non-autotuned kernel
@triton.jit
def test_kernel(
    query_ptr, kv_cache_ptr, block_table_ptr, seq_lens_ptr, output_ptr,
    B, H, Hkv, D, max_blocks, P,
    stride_q_b, stride_q_h,
    stride_kv_blk, stride_kv_pos, stride_kv_h,
    stride_bt_b,
    BLOCK_L: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, group_size: tl.constexpr,
):
    pid_b = tl.program_id(0)
    pid_kv = tl.program_id(1)
    seq_len = tl.load(seq_lens_ptr + pid_b)
    num_pages = (seq_len + P - 1) // P
    offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D)
    offs_l = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_L)
    offs_g = tl.arange(0, group_size)
    
    q_head_base = pid_kv * group_size
    q_offs = pid_b * stride_q_b + (q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_q_h + offs_d[None, :]
    mask_q = offs_d[None, :] < D
    q = tl.load(query_ptr + q_offs, mask=mask_q, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
    
    m = tl.full([group_size], float('-inf'), dtype=tl.float32)
    l_sum = tl.zeros([group_size], dtype=tl.float32)
    acc = tl.zeros([group_size, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32)
    scale = 1.0 / tl.sqrt(D.to(tl.float32))
    
    for page_idx in range(num_pages):
        blk_idx = tl.load(block_table_ptr + pid_b * stride_bt_b + page_idx)
        if page_idx == num_pages - 1:
            rem = seq_len % P
            tokens_this_page = tl.where(rem == 0, P, rem)
        else:
            tokens_this_page = P
        
        for l_start in range(0, P, BLOCK_L):
            l_offs = l_start + offs_l
            valid_l = l_offs < tokens_this_page
            
            k_base = blk_idx.to(tl.int64) * stride_kv_blk + pid_kv * stride_kv_h
            
            k_offs = k_base + l_offs[:, None] * stride_kv_pos + offs_d[None, :]
            mask_2d = valid_l[:, None] & (offs_d[None, :] < D)
            k_tile = tl.load(kv_cache_ptr + k_offs, mask=mask_2d, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
            
            scores = tl.dot(q, tl.trans(k_tile)) * scale
            scores = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], scores, float('-inf'))
            
            m_new = tl.maximum(m, tl.max(scores, axis=1))
            rescale = tl.exp(m - m_new)
            acc = acc * rescale[:, None]
            l_sum = l_sum * rescale
            
            p = tl.exp(scores - m_new[:, None])
            p = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], p, 0.0)
            l_sum = l_sum + tl.sum(p, axis=1)
            
            v_offs = k_base + D + l_offs[:, None] * stride_kv_pos + offs_d[None, :]
            v_tile = tl.load(kv_cache_ptr + v_offs, mask=mask_2d, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
            acc = acc + tl.dot(p, v_tile)
            m = m_new
    
    out_offs = pid_b * stride_q_b + (q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_q_h + offs_d[None, :]
    out_vals = acc / l_sum[:, None]
    tl.store(output_ptr + out_offs, out_vals.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_q)

        q, kvc, bt, sl = inputs
        B, H, D = q.shape
        Hkv = kvc.shape[2]
        P = kvc.shape[1]
        group_size = H // Hkv
