Those values could be anything, but they're masked in the computation. Wait, but this IS the BLOCK_L > P bug I identified earlier! l_offs beyond P-1 read from wrong memory locations. For positions 16-31, the memory offset is: ``` k_base + 16 * stride_kv_pos + offs_d ``` But stride_kv_pos is the stride of the position dimension, which is Hkv * 2*D. Position 16 in a page doesn't exist — it would be in the NEXT page (since each page only has P=16 positions). So position 16 would actually read from `blk_idx * stride_kv_blk + 16 * stride_kv_pos`, which is `blk_idx * (P * Hkv * 2*D) + 16 * (Hkv * 2*D)` = `(blk_idx * P + 16) * Hkv * 2*D`. This is position 16 of block blk_idx, which is actually position 0 of block (blk_idx + 1)! So we're reading from the WRONG page entirely. This could cause NaN because the wrong data might have very large values that overflow exp(). Wait, but valid_l masks out positions 16-31 in the scores computation, so they don't affect the attention weights or output. However, the K and V tiles are LOADED from memory (including the wrong data), and the `tl.dot(q, k_f32)` includes positions 16-31 in the multiplication (even though they're later masked by `tl.where(valid_l[None, :], scores, float('-inf'))`). So the dot product for positions 16-31 is computed using wrong K data and wrong V data, but then masked out. The issue is that the LOAD from global memory reads from the wrong addresses, which could cause: 1. Reading out-of-bounds memory (unlikely since kv_cache has plenty of pages) 2. Reading data from the wrong page that happens to have very large values, causing overflow in fp32 dot product Actually, the fp32 dot product shouldn't overflow easily — fp32 can handle values up to ~3.4e38, and the dot product of 128 bf16 values (each ≤ ~3e4) is at most 128 * 3e4 * 3e4 ≈ 1.15e11 — well within fp32 range. So overflow in the dot product is not the issue. Then the scores (dot product * scale) are at most ~1e10 for wrong data. exp(1e10) definitely overflows fp32! So if the wrong memory locations happen to contain large values, the exp would overflow → inf → NaN in subsequent operations. Wait, but the scores for invalid positions are set to -inf: ```python scores = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], scores, float("-inf")) ``` And then: ```python m_new = tl.maximum(m, tl.max(scores, axis=1)) ``` The max of scores ignores -inf values (since at least one valid position exists). So m_new should be correct. Then: ```python p = tl.exp(scores - m_new[:, None]) p = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], p, 0.0) ``` For invalid positions: scores = -inf, scores - m_new = -inf - some_finite = -inf, exp(-inf) = 0. Then `tl.where(valid_l, p, 0.0)` = 0.0 for invalid positions. OK. So even with wrong data loaded, if masking is correct, the NaN shouldn't happen. Hmm, but wait — the issue is specific to the `large_q_kv` case. Let me check if the autotuner selected BLOCK_L=32 for this case. If BLOCK_L=32: - BLOCK_L > P (32 > 16) - The tile `l_start=0, l_offs=[0..31]` loads positions 0-31 from page 0 - Positions 16-31 are in page 1 (wrong page!) - But the first iteration of the page loop is for page 0 only Wait, I misunderstood the kernel structure. Each iteration of the page loop processes ONE page. The tile loop within is `for l_start in range(0, P, BLOCK_L)`. For P=16 and BLOCK_L=32: this loop runs only once with l_start=0. Then l_offs = [0..31]. We load K and V for positions 0-31 from THIS page (blk_idx). But positions 16-31 DON'T belong to this page — they'd be at `base + 16*stride_kv_pos`, which is the memory location for position 16 of this page. But this page only has positions 0-15. The memory at position 16 is actually position 0 of the NEXT page (since layout is (num_blocks, P, Hkv, 2*D), the next position after P-1 is the next page). But we're loading them anyway! And the LOAD happens from the wrong addresses. Even though we mask them in the computation, the load itself might trigger issues (though it shouldn't — we're loading from valid allocated memory since there are many pages). Actually, wait. For this to cause NaN in the output, the wrong values would need to: 1. Be loaded into K_tile and V_tile 2. Be included in dot products 3. Cause numerical issues that propagate But looking at the code: ```python scores = tl.dot(q, tl.trans(k_tile)) * scale scores = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], scores, float("-inf")) ``` The dot product includes ALL positions in K_tile (including wrong ones). But then invalid positions are set to -inf. So the invalid dot products don't affect max (since valid ones exist) or softmax (since exp(-inf) = 0). The V accumulation: ```python p = tl.exp(scores - m_new[:, None]) p = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], p, 0.0) acc = acc + tl.dot(p, v_tile) ``` Invalid positions have p=0, so they contribute nothing to acc. The V_tile data for invalid positions is multiplied by 0. Fine. So the BLOCK_L > P issue shouldn't cause NaN by itself. The NaN must come from somewhere else. Let me look at the large_q_kv case more carefully. The input is scaled by 8.0, so Q and KV values are 8x the nominal. The scores = Q @ K^T * scale are 64x larger. The max score could be quite large. Actually, let me verify: what's the maximum possible Q @ K^T value with bf16 inputs? Q and K are random values with std 0.1 (in the nominal case). For the large case, they're scaled by 8 → std 0.8. The dot product over D=128 elements: each product has mean ~0 (since independent random), and the sum of 128 such products has std ~ sqrt(128) * (0.8 * 0.8) ≈ 11.3 * 0.64 ≈ 7.23. The maximum over L=1024 positions could be ~4*7.23 ≈ 29. Then `scores = dot * scale = dot / sqrt(128)` ≈ 29 / 11.3 ≈ 2.56. Not large enough to overflow exp. Hmm, but what about larger shape? Let me check shape 3 (L=4096, D=128) with group_size=8. The max score over 4096 positions could be ~4.5*7.23 ≈ 32.5, and scores ≈ 32.5 / 11.3 ≈ 2.87. Still fine. What about extremely rare values? With 1024 positions * 32 heads * 8 batch = 262,144 dot products, the max could be higher. Using extreme value theory, the max of 262K independent samples from N(0, 7.23) could be around 7.23 * sqrt(2*log(262144)) ≈ 7.23 * 5.0 ≈ 36. So scores ≈ 36/11.3 ≈ 3.2. exp(3.2) ≈ 24.5. Fine. So the overflow theory for large_q_kv doesn't hold. The NaN must come from somewhere else. Let me check if maybe there's a division by zero. After all pages are processed: ```python out_vals = acc / l_sum[:, None] ``` If l_sum is 0 for some Q head, this is 0/0 = NaN. When would l_sum be 0? If all scores are -inf for all tiles. This happens if `valid_l` is all False for every tile. This would happen if `tokens_this_page = 0` for all pages. Can tokens_this_page be 0? For the last page: ```python rem = seq_len % P tokens_this_page = tl.where(rem == 0, P, rem) ``` If seq_len % P == 0, rem = 0, and tl.where(0 == 0, P, 0) = P. Not 0. But what if seq_len is a multiple of P? Say seq_len = 1024, P = 16, rem = 0, tokens_this_page = P = 16. Fine. Hmm, but wait. What if seq_len is 0? Then num_pages = (0 + 15) // 16 = 0. The page loop doesn't execute. m = -inf, l_sum = 0. Then `acc / l_sum` = 0/0 = NaN. But seq_lens shouldn't be 0 for a valid test case. The default seq_len values are all positive. OK, I'm going in circles. Let me just write the debug script to file and run it. Actually, I realize I should focus on fixing the known issues: 1. BLOCK_L > P causes wrong loads (remove BLOCK_L=32) 2. BLOCK_D < D causes incomplete dot products (remove BLOCK_D=64 for D=128) And then see if the NaN issue goes away. Also, I should handle the case where l_sum could be 0 (defensive programming). Let me fix these issues and re-test.

+"""Debug script to compare ref vs sol for specific cases."""
+import sys
+sys.path.insert(0, '/workspace')
+import torch
+import reference
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+from src.eval.numeric_stress import numeric_stress_cases, numeric_stress_context, tolerance_for_case
+from src.eval.correctness import check_correctness
+import shapes, yaml
+from pathlib import Path
+
+meta = yaml.safe_load(Path('problem.yaml').read_text())
+device = torch.device('cuda:0')
+
+# Non-autotuned kernel with proper D handling
+@triton.jit
+def debug_kernel(
+    query_ptr, kv_cache_ptr, block_table_ptr, seq_lens_ptr, output_ptr,
+    B, H, Hkv, D, max_blocks, P,
+    stride_q_b, stride_q_h,
+    stride_kv_blk, stride_kv_pos, stride_kv_h,
+    stride_bt_b,
+    BLOCK_L: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, group_size: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    pid_b = tl.program_id(0)
+    pid_kv = tl.program_id(1)
+    seq_len = tl.load(seq_lens_ptr + pid_b)
+    num_pages = (seq_len + P - 1) // P
+    offs_l = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_L)
+    offs_g = tl.arange(0, group_size)
+    
+    q_head_base = pid_kv * group_size
+    
+    m = tl.full([group_size], float('-inf'), dtype=tl.float32)
+    l_sum = tl.zeros([group_size], dtype=tl.float32)
+    acc = tl.zeros([group_size, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32)
+    scale = 1.0 / tl.sqrt(D.to(tl.float32))
+    
+    for page_idx in range(num_pages):
+        blk_idx = tl.load(block_table_ptr + pid_b * stride_bt_b + page_idx)
+        if page_idx == num_pages - 1:
+            rem = seq_len % P
+            tokens_this_page = tl.where(rem == 0, P, rem)
+        else:
+            tokens_this_page = P
+        
+        for l_start in range(0, P, BLOCK_L):
+            l_offs = l_start + offs_l
+            valid_l = l_offs < tokens_this_page
+            k_base = blk_idx.to(tl.int64) * stride_kv_blk + pid_kv * stride_kv_h
+            
+            # Process D in BLOCK_D chunks, accumulating the full dot product
+            scores_sum = tl.zeros([group_size, BLOCK_L], dtype=tl.float32)
+            acc_update = tl.zeros([group_size, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32)
+            
+            for d_start in range(0, D, BLOCK_D):
+                offs_d = d_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D)
+                mask_d = offs_d < D
+                
+                # Load Q chunk
+                q_offs = pid_b * stride_q_b + (q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_q_h + offs_d[None, :]
+                q_chunk = tl.load(query_ptr + q_offs, mask=mask_d[None, :], other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
+                
+                # Load K chunk
+                k_offs = k_base + l_offs[:, None] * stride_kv_pos + offs_d[None, :]
+                mask_2d = valid_l[:, None] & mask_d[None, :]
+                k_chunk = tl.load(kv_cache_ptr + k_offs, mask=mask_2d, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
+                
+                # Partial dot product
+                scores_sum += tl.dot(q_chunk, tl.trans(k_chunk))
+            
+            scores = scores_sum * scale
+            scores = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], scores, float('-inf'))
+            
+            m_new = tl.maximum(m, tl.max(scores, axis=1))
+            rescale = tl.exp(m - m_new)
+            acc = acc * rescale[:, None]
+            l_sum = l_sum * rescale
+            
+            p = tl.exp(scores - m_new[:, None])
+            p = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], p, 0.0)
+            l_sum = l_sum + tl.sum(p, axis=1)
+            
+            # Accumulate V (also in D chunks)
+            for d_start in range(0, D, BLOCK_D):
+                offs_d = d_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D)
+                mask_d = offs_d < D
+                
+                v_offs = k_base + D + l_offs[:, None] * stride_kv_pos + offs_d[None, :]
+                mask_2d = valid_l[:, None] & mask_d[None, :]
+                v_chunk = tl.load(kv_cache_ptr + v_offs, mask=mask_2d, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
+                
+                acc_update_d = tl.dot(p, v_chunk)  # (group_size, BLOCK_D)
+                # Scatter into acc — only valid D positions
+                acc_d = tl.where(mask_d[None, :], acc_update_d, 0.0)
+                acc += acc_d
+            
+            m = m_new
+    
+    # Write output (in D chunks)
+    for d_start in range(0, D, BLOCK_D):
+        offs_d = d_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D)
+        mask_d = offs_d < D
+        
+        out_offs = pid_b * stride_q_b + (q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_q_h + offs_d[None, :]
+        
+        # Extract the right portion of acc
+        acc_chunk = tl.zeros([group_size, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32)
+        # Hmm, this is tricky — acc has shape (group_size, BLOCK_D) but we need
+        # to extract the d_start..d_start+BLOCK_D portion.
+        # Actually acc stores the full D-dim accumulation, but padded to BLOCK_D.
+        # Since BLOCK_D divides D (or we have multiple chunks), this gets complex.
+        
+        out_vals = acc / l_sum[:, None]
+        tl.store(output_ptr + out_offs, out_vals.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_d[None, :])
+
+
+# Test nominal case
+shape = shapes.SHAPES[0]
+reference.BATCH = shape['batch']

+reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape['num_kv_heads']

"""Debug script to compare ref vs sol for specific cases."""
import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '/workspace')
import torch
import reference
import triton
import triton.language as tl
from src.eval.numeric_stress import numeric_stress_cases, numeric_stress_context, tolerance_for_case
from src.eval.correctness import check_correctness
import shapes, yaml
from pathlib import Path

meta = yaml.safe_load(Path('problem.yaml').read_text())
device = torch.device('cuda:0')

# Simple kernel: BLOCK_D exactly equals D, no chunking needed
@triton.jit
def debug_kernel(
    query_ptr, kv_cache_ptr, block_table_ptr, seq_lens_ptr, output_ptr,
    B, H, Hkv, D, max_blocks, P,
    stride_q_b, stride_q_h,
    stride_kv_blk, stride_kv_pos, stride_kv_h,
    stride_bt_b,
    BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, group_size: tl.constexpr,
):
    pid_b = tl.program_id(0)
    pid_kv = tl.program_id(1)
    seq_len = tl.load(seq_lens_ptr + pid_b)
    num_pages = (seq_len + P - 1) // P
    offs_l = tl.arange(0, P)  # exactly PAGE_SIZE positions per tile
    offs_g = tl.arange(0, group_size)
    offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D)
    
    q_head_base = pid_kv * group_size
    
    m = tl.full([group_size], float('-inf'), dtype=tl.float32)
    l_sum = tl.zeros([group_size], dtype=tl.float32)
    acc = tl.zeros([group_size, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32)
    scale = 1.0 / tl.sqrt(D.to(tl.float32))
    
    for page_idx in range(num_pages):
        blk_idx = tl.load(block_table_ptr + pid_b * stride_bt_b + page_idx)
        if page_idx == num_pages - 1:
            rem = seq_len % P
            tokens_this_page = tl.where(rem == 0, P, rem)
        else:
            tokens_this_page = P
        
        l_offs = offs_l
        valid_l = l_offs < tokens_this_page
        k_base = blk_idx.to(tl.int64) * stride_kv_blk + pid_kv * stride_kv_h
        
        # Load Q
        mask_q = offs_d[None, :] < D
        q_offs = pid_b * stride_q_b + (q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_q_h + offs_d[None, :]
        q = tl.load(query_ptr + q_offs, mask=mask_q, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
        
        # Load K
        k_offs = k_base + l_offs[:, None] * stride_kv_pos + offs_d[None, :]
        mask_k = valid_l[:, None] & (offs_d[None, :] < D)
        k_tile = tl.load(kv_cache_ptr + k_offs, mask=mask_k, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
        
        scores = tl.dot(q, tl.trans(k_tile)) * scale
        scores = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], scores, float('-inf'))
        
        m_new = tl.maximum(m, tl.max(scores, axis=1))
        rescale = tl.exp(m - m_new)
        acc = acc * rescale[:, None]
        l_sum = l_sum * rescale
        
        p = tl.exp(scores - m_new[:, None])
        p = tl.where(valid_l[None, :], p, 0.0)
        l_sum = l_sum + tl.sum(p, axis=1)
        
        # Load V
        v_offs = k_base + D + l_offs[:, None] * stride_kv_pos + offs_d[None, :]
        v_tile = tl.load(kv_cache_ptr + v_offs, mask=mask_k, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
        acc = acc + tl.dot(p, v_tile)
        
        m = m_new
    
    out_offs = pid_b * stride_q_b + (q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_q_h + offs_d[None, :]
    out_vals = acc / l_sum[:, None]
    tl.store(output_ptr + out_offs, out_vals.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_q)

The issue is that `P` is a runtime argument, not a `tl.constexpr`. `tl.arange` requires constexpr start and end values. In my original kernel, I used `offs_l = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_L)` where BLOCK_L is a constexpr. But `P` is not constexpr. I need to revert to using BLOCK_L (which IS constexpr) and ensure that BLOCK_L ≤ P. Since P is always 16, I can just use BLOCK_L=16. But for the tile loop, I was using `for l_start in range(0, P, BLOCK_L)`. Since P is not constexpr, but the loop range should be fine since P is determined at kernel launch time. Actually, `tl.arange(0, P)` fails because P needs to be constexpr. But `tl.arange(0, BLOCK_L)` works because BLOCK_L is constexpr. So I should use BLOCK_L=16 for the tile size and ensure it's ≤ P (which it always is since P=16). Wait, but in my original kernel I had: ```python offs_l = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_L) for l_start in range(0, P, BLOCK_L): l_offs = l_start + offs_l ``` Here `l_start` is a python-level variable from the triton-level `range(0, P, BLOCK_L)`. But P is a triton scalar, not a python int. Can `range(0, P, BLOCK_L)` work with triton scalars? Actually, in Triton JIT, `range` with triton scalar arguments should work for dynamic loops. The loop variable `l_start` becomes a triton scalar. But `l_offs = l_start + offs_l` — adding a triton scalar to a triton tensor should broadcast correctly. The issue in my debug kernel was `tl.arange(0, P)` where P is not constexpr. Let me fix this. Also, let me simplify: since P is always 16, just use BLOCK_L = 16 as a constexpr.  """Paged attention decode kernel for RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell).
 
 Triton-based decode kernel with online softmax and paged KV-cache gathering.
+Uses one block per (batch, kv_head) pair, processes all GQA query heads together.
 """
 
 import math
@@ -24,40 +25,56 @@
 # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
 # Triton kernel
 # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+#
+# Design:
+#   - Grid: (batch, num_kv_heads)
+#   - Each program handles one GQA group (group_size query heads sharing
+#     one KV head) for one batch element.
+#   - Q heads are loaded together and all dot products computed vectorised.
+#   - KV pages are iterated one at a time (PAGE_SIZE positions each).
+#   - Online softmax (fp32) prevents materialising the full attention matrix.
+#   - BLOCK_D is set to D at launch time so no dimension chunking is needed.
+#   - BLOCK_L is exactly PAGE_SIZE (16) — a single page fits in one tile.
 
 
 @triton.autotune(
     configs=[
-        triton.Config({"BLOCK_L": 16, "BLOCK_D": 64}, num_warps=4),
-        triton.Config({"BLOCK_L": 16, "BLOCK_D": 64}, num_warps=8),
-        triton.Config({"BLOCK_L": 16, "BLOCK_D": 128}, num_warps=4),

The benchmark passes (RESULT: OK with peak_fraction=0.2343, above the 0.1 threshold). But the performance is not great for all shapes. Let me analyze: - Shape 0 (B=8, H=32, Hkv=8, D=128, L=1024): 381 GB/s, 21.2% peak — OK but not great - Shape 1 (B=32, H=32, Hkv=8, D=128, L=2048): 1311 GB/s, 72.9% peak — Excellent! - Shape 2 (B=4, H=64, Hkv=8, D=128, L=4096): 246 GB/s, 13.6% peak — Poor - Shape 3 (B=16, H=32, Hkv=8, D=128, L=1535): 787 GB/s, 43.7% peak — OK - Shape 4 (B=8, H=16, Hkv=4, D=64, L=2000): 138 GB/s, 7.7% peak — Poor The gmean is 0.2343, which is above 0.1 but there's room for improvement. The poor performers are: - Shape 2 (B=4, H=64, Hkv=8, D=128, L=4096): only 4 * 8 = 32 thread blocks. With 188 SMs, that's severe underutilization. Each block processes 4096 tokens × 8 Q heads = significant work, but most SMs are idle. - Shape 4 (B=8, H=16, Hkv=4, D=64, L=2000): 8 * 4 = 32 thread blocks. Same underutilization issue plus D=64 means smaller dot products. The main issue is insufficient parallelism — the grid size is (batch, num_kv_heads), which for small batches gives too few blocks. Solutions: 1. Split along the sequence dimension: use (batch, num_kv_heads, seq_chunks) grid 2. Split along the Q head dimension: use (batch, num_heads) grid For option 2 (per-Q-head), the issue is that multiple Q heads in the same GQA group would redundantly load the same K/V data. But with the L2 cache, this redundancy is mitigated. For option 1 (seq-chunking), I'd need a two-pass approach with cross-block reduction for the online softmax. This is more complex but eliminates the redundant loads. Let me try option 2 first — it's simpler and should improve parallelism significantly. Grid: (B, H) — one block per Q head (not per KV head) Each block handles exactly one Q head. The KV head is q_head // group_size. All blocks for Q heads in the same GQA group load the same K/V data, but the L2 cache should serve most of these from cache. The per-block work is simpler since we only have one Q head's state: ``` m = -inf, l = 0, acc = zeros(D) for each page: load K_tile, V_tile (same as before, but for one Q head) scores = q @ K_tile^T * scale update m, l, acc output = acc / l ``` This should give us: - Shape 0: 8*32=256 blocks (good) - Shape 1: 32*32=1024 blocks (great) - Shape 2: 4*64=256 blocks (good) - Shape 3: 16*32=512 blocks (great) - Shape 4: 8*16=128 blocks (decent) Let me implement this. The main change is the grid and the Q head handling. Actually, wait — with one block per Q head and group_size > 1, multiple blocks access the SAME K/V pages. This redundant loading wastes memory bandwidth. For a memory-bound kernel, this might hurt performance even with L2 caching. Let me think about this trade-off: - With grid=(B, Hkv): group_size=4 Q heads per block, no redundant K/V loads, but fewer blocks - With grid=(B, H): 1 Q head per block, group_size× redundant K/V loads, but more blocks For shape 2 (B=4, Hkv=8, group_size=8): - Grid (B, Hkv): 32 blocks, each loads K/V once for 8 Q heads - Grid (B, H): 256 blocks, each loads K/V for 1 Q head, 8× redundancy With 256 blocks on 188 SMs (good occupancy), the L2 cache hit rate for K/V should be high. The first time a page is loaded by any block, it goes to L2. Subsequent loads by other blocks in the same GQA group should hit L2. But on Blackwell with 96MB L2 cache, can we fit the full KV cache? For shape 2: each batch element has 4096 tokens × 8 KV heads × (128*2) = 8,388,608 bf16 values = ~16.8 MB. For 4 batch elements = ~67 MB. That fits in a 96 MB L2 cache! So L2 caching should be very effective. The redundant loads are essentially free (L2 bandwidth is much higher than DRAM bandwidth). Let me switch to grid=(B, H) for better parallelism. But I also want to keep the group_size=1 optimization for the kernel — simpler code and less register pressure (only one Q head's state to track). Actually, let me try an intermediate approach: grid=(B, H) but still process Q heads in groups within each block. No, that makes no sense. Let me go with grid=(B, H). Each block handles one Q head. The kernel is simpler. For the kernel, with grid=(B, H): - pid = program_id(0), we can compute: b = pid // H, q_head = pid % H - Or use 2D grid: (H, B) with program_id(0) = q_head, program_id(1) = b Let me use 2D grid (H, B): - pid_q = program_id(0) = q_head index - pid_b = program_id(1) = batch index - kv_head = pid_q // group_size The kernel computes attention for a single Q head. The code is much simpler. But wait, there's a concern: shape 1 has 32*32 = 1024 blocks. Each block processes 2048 tokens. With 256 threads per block, each thread handles 8 tokens (assuming each token's dot product is done by the whole block). That's not bad. Actually, with one Q head per block, I can use fewer threads per block (e.g., 128 threads = 4 warps). For D=128, each thread handles D/32 = 4 elements per warp. For D=64, each thread handles 2 elements. The kernel would be: ``` pid_q = program_id(0), pid_b = program_id(1) kv_head = pid_q // group_size Load Q[pid_b, pid_q, :] Iterate over pages for batch pid_b, KV head kv_head Online softmax with one Q head Write output[pid_b, pid_q, :] ``` Let me implement this. I'll still load the entire page at once (16 positions × D elements), but now only one Q head participates in the dot product. Wait, but a key question: should I still use tl.dot (which is optimized for matrix multiplication) or just compute the dot products manually? With one Q head, tl.dot of (1, D) @ (D, 16) → (1, 16) is a GEMV. Triton's tl.dot should still be efficient for this. But hold on — with only one Q head, the tl.dot operation is (1, D) @ (D, 16) which has very low arithmetic intensity. Most of the time is spent loading K and V from memory. So the kernel is still memory-bound, and having more blocks helps hide memory latency. Let me implement this now.

Wait, there's a potential issue with the partial results. In the pass 1 kernel, I'm storing the partial results per Q head. But multiple programs within the same grid process different Q heads in the same GQA group. They all try to write to different locations in the partial output tensor, so there shouldn't be conflicts. But there's a subtle bug: each program in grid (num_chunks, B, Hkv) processes group_size Q heads. It stores partial results for all group_size Q heads. But another program at a different `pid_kv` might also store to different Q heads. Since each (pid_c, pid_b, pid_kv) combination writes to unique Q heads, there should be no conflicts, right? Wait, the partial output is indexed as: ``` partial_out_ptr + c * stride_po_c + b * stride_po_b + q_head * stride_po_h + ... ``` Each program (c, b, kv) writes to Q heads `kv * group_size + g` for g in 0..group_size-1. Different programs have different kv values, so they write to different Q heads. No conflicts. Good. But wait, I'm writing both `m[g]` and `l_sum[g]` as bf16 values. These are float32 values being cast to bf16 when stored. The reduction kernel reads them as bf16 and converts to float32. This could lose precision, especially for m (which can be -inf) and l_sum (which can be very small or large). Actually, the bigger issue is that `m[g]` can be -inf (if all scores are masked), and storing -inf in bf16 might not survive the round-trip. Let me check: bf16 supports -inf (exponent all 1s, mantissa 0). So -inf should be preserved. But l_sum could underflow to 0 when stored as bf16. If l_sum is very small (e.g., from a chunk with very negative scores), it could be truncated to 0. This would cause the reduction to ignore that chunk's contribution. To be safe, I should store the partial results in fp32. But the partial output tensor is bf16 for memory efficiency. Let me change it to fp32. Actually wait, there's another issue. In the store: ```python tl.store(..., acc[g, :].to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_d) ``` The acc values are fp32 and I'm converting to bf16. The m and l values are also fp32 but stored directly (implicitly converted to the output tensor's dtype). Since the tensor is bf16, they'll be truncated. Let me use fp32 for the intermediate tensor. The memory cost is 2x but correctness is more important. Actually, there's an even simpler approach. Let me not store partial results for the reduction at all. Instead, just use the 2-pass approach where pass 1 computes partial results that are combined in pass 2, but store them in fp32. Let me change the intermediate tensor to fp32 and also fix the store of m and l to use proper conversion. Wait, actually, I realize there's another fundamental issue with my partial reduction. When storing m and l: ```python tl.store(partial_out_ptr + ... + D, m[g]) tl.store(partial_out_ptr + ... + D + 1, l_sum[g]) ``` These are fp32 values stored into a bf16 tensor. The m value could be -inf, which in bf16 is 0xFF80. The l_sum value could be 0, or very large. For the reduction to work correctly, I need m and l to be precise enough. Using fp32 for the intermediate tensor would fix this. Actually wait, there's a more fundamental design issue. The partial results combine information from all chunks into a final answer. If chunk c has m_c = -inf and l_c = 0 (all scores were -inf, i.e., no valid tokens in this chunk), the reduction should ignore it. My code handles this with `chunk_valid = l_c > 0.0`. But l_c is stored in bf16, and a very small but non-zero l_c could be truncated to 0, causing that chunk to be ignored when it shouldn't be. For robustness, let me use fp32 for the partial tensor. The memory cost is minimal (a few MB at most). Let me update the code. But there's also a correctness concern with the reduction formula. Let me verify: For two chunks with partial results (m1, l1, o1) and (m2, l2, o2): - m1 = max score in chunk 1, l1 = sum(exp(scores1 - m1)), o1 = sum(exp(scores1 - m1) * V1) - Similarly for chunk 2 The combined result: - m = max(m1, m2) - l = l1 * exp(m1 - m) + l2 * exp(m2 - m) - o = o1 * exp(m1 - m) + o2 * exp(m2 - m) - output = o / l Let me verify against the sequential online softmax: First process chunk 1: m=m1, l=l1, o=o1 Then process chunk 2: m_new = max(m1, m2) rescale = exp(m1 - m_new) = exp(m1 - m) o = o1 * rescale l = l1 * rescale p = exp(scores2 - m_new) = exp(scores2 - m) l += sum(p) = l + exp(m2 - m) * sum(exp(scores2 - m2)) = l + exp(m2 - m) * l2 o += p @ V2 = o + exp(m2 - m) * sum(exp(scores2 - m2) * V2) = o + exp(m2 - m) * o2 So the final result: - o = o1 * exp(m1 - m) + o2 * exp(m2 - m) - l = l1 * exp(m1 - m) + l2 * exp(m2 - m) Where m = max(m1, m2). This matches my reduction formula. But wait, there's an issue with my implementation. Since the partial tensor is fp32, each element is 4 bytes. But Triton uses element-wise offsets (not byte offsets), so this should be correct. Similarly for l_sum at offset D+1. And for acc at offsets 0..D-1. Let me rewrite the store section: ```python # Store m for all Q heads in this group m_offs = ( pid_c * stride_po_c + pid_b * stride_po_b + (q_head_base + offs_g) * stride_po_h + D ) tl.store(partial_out_ptr + m_offs, m) # Store l_sum for all Q heads l_offs = ( pid_c * stride_po_c + pid_b * stride_po_b + (q_head_base + offs_g) * stride_po_h + D + 1 ) tl.store(partial_out_ptr + l_offs, l_sum) # Store acc for all Q heads # acc: (group_size, BLOCK_D), need to store at offsets 0..D-1 for each Q head acc_offs = ( pid_c * stride_po_c + pid_b * stride_po_b + (q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_po_h + offs_d[None, :] ) mask_acc = offs_d[None, :] < D tl.store(partial_out_ptr + acc_offs, acc, mask=mask_acc) ``` This writes all Q heads' data in 3 vectorized store operations instead of a loop. The key change: `(q_head_base + offs_g[:, None]) * stride_po_h + offs_d[None, :]` creates a 2D offset grid of shape (group_size, BLOCK_D). Combined with the batch/chunk offsets, this writes the acc data for all Q heads at once. Let me make this change now.

reasoning (8664 chars)
The benchmark shows great performance improvement (39.7% peak, up from 23.4%), but the correctness check fails on the nominal case. The max_abs_diff is 3.05 which is huge — basically all outputs are wrong. The issue is likely in the pass 2 reduction. Let me debug. Looking at the error more carefully: `max_abs_diff=3.05029`. This is enormous for bf16 values that should be in range [-0.01, 0.01]. Something is fundamentally broken in the reduction. Let me think about what could go wrong... 1. The partial output strides might be wrong 2. The reduction formula might have a bug 3. The chunk boundary handling might be wrong 4. The `num_chunks` calculation might be incorrect Wait, I notice something: in the pass 1 kernel, I use `num_pages_total` (passed as a kernel arg) but it's actually `max_pages` from the host. The host computes: ```python max_seq = int(seq_lens.max().item()) max_pages = (max_seq + P - 1) // P ``` But in the kernel, `num_pages` is computed per batch element as `(seq_len + P - 1) // P`. These could differ if different batch elements have different seq_lens. But in the test inputs, all batch elements have the same seq_len. Actually, there's a bigger issue. Let me trace through the nominal case (shape 0: B=8, H=32, Hkv=8, D=128, L=1024, P=16): - max_pages = (1024 + 15) / 16 = 64 - base_blocks = B * Hkv = 8 * 8 = 64 - target_blocks = 256 - num_chunks = max(1, min(64, 256 // 64)) = max(1, min(64, 4)) = 4 - pages_per_chunk = (64 + 3) / 4 = 16 So there are 4 chunks, each with 16 pages. Grid: (4, 8, 8) = 256 blocks. That's fine. In the pass 1 kernel, chunk 0 processes pages 0-15, chunk 1 processes 16-31, etc. The partial results for each chunk are written to `partial[c, b, q_head, ...]`. In the pass 2 kernel, for each (q_head, b), it reads partial results from all chunks and combines them. Let me trace the reduction more carefully. For Q head 0, batch 0, chunk 0: - Process pages 0-15: compute partial (m_0, l_0, acc_0) - Store at partial[0, 0, 0, :] For chunk 1: pages 16-31, store at partial[1, 0, 0, :] For chunk 2: pages 32-47, store at partial[2, 0, 0, :] For chunk 3: pages 48-63, store at partial[3, 0, 0, :] Then in pass 2, for Q head 0, batch 0: - c=0: m_c = partial[0, 0, 0, D] = partial[0, 0, 0, 128], l_c = partial[0, 0, 0, 129] - c=1: m_c = partial[1, 0, 0, 128], l_c = partial[1, 0, 0, 129] - ... Wait, the partial tensor shape is (num_chunks, B, H, D+2). So: - stride_pi_c = partial.stride(0) = B * H * (D+2) - stride_pi_b = partial.stride(1) = H * (D+2) - stride_pi_h = partial.stride(2) = D+2 For accessing `partial[c, b, q_head, d]`: - offset = c * stride_pi_c + b * stride_pi_b + q_head * stride_pi_h + d For `partial[c, b, q_head, D]` (the m value): - offset = c * (B * H * (D+2)) + b * (H * (D+2)) + q_head * (D+2) + D Let me verify with num_chunks=4, B=8, H=32, D=128, D+2=130: - stride_pi_c = 8 * 32 * 130 = 33280 - stride_pi_b = 32 * 130 = 4160 - stride_pi_h = 130 In the pass 1 kernel, for chunk 0, batch 0, kv_head=0: - q_head_base = 0 * 4 = 0 - Q heads: 0, 1, 2, 3 - Store m[g] at offset: c * stride_po_c + b * stride_po_b + (q_head_base + g) * stride_po_h + D - For g=0: 0 * 33280 + 0 * 4160 + 0 * 130 + 128 = 128. Element 128 in the fp32 tensor. That's partial[0, 0, 0, 128]. ✓ - For g=1: 0 * 33280 + 0 * 4160 + 1 * 130 + 128 = 258. Hmm, actually, in Triton, `tl.store` with a 1D pointer does a vectorized store where each thread stores one element. But the offset computation needs to be per-thread. With `m_offs` being a tensor of offsets, each thread computes its own offset and stores the corresponding value. Wait, but which thread stores which element? In Triton, a 1D tensor like `m` of shape (group_size,) is distributed across threads. Each thread handles some elements of the tensor. The offset tensor `m_offs` has the same shape and is also distributed. So thread i computes offset `m_offs[i]` and stores value `m[i]`. This should work correctly for scatter stores. Interesting! The partial decode pass 1 is CORRECT (max abs diff = 3.29e-5, well within tolerance). The problem is in the pass 2 reduction kernel. Even with num_chunks=1 (no actual reduction needed), the reduction pass produces garbage. This means the reduction kernel is loading the partial data incorrectly or the reduction formula is broken. Let me trace through the reduction for a single chunk: In pass 2, for Q head q, batch b: - c=0: Load m_c = partial[0, b, q, D], l_c = partial[0, b, q, D+1] - m_global starts at -inf - m_new = max(-inf, m_c) = m_c - l_c > 0.0 → chunk_valid = True - rescale_old = exp(m_global - m_new) = exp(-inf - m_c) = exp(-inf) = 0 - rescale_c = exp(m_c - m_new) = exp(m_c - m_c) = exp(0) = 1 - o_global = 0 * 0 + 1 * l_c * acc_c = l_c * acc_c - l_global = 0 * 0 + 1 * l_c = l_c - m_global = m_new = m_c Then output = o_global / l_global = (l_c * acc_c) / l_c = acc_c. But wait — acc_c is the WEIGHTED sum, not the normalized output. In the partial pass, `acc` stores the weighted sum of V values: ``` acc += sum_t exp(scores[t] - m_global) * V[t] ``` And `l_sum` stores: ``` l_sum += sum_t exp(scores[t] - m_global) ``` The correct output is `acc / l_sum`. In the reduction: ``` o_global = l_c * acc_c (where acc_c is the partial weighted sum) l_global = l_c output = o_global / l_global = (l_c * acc_c) / l_c = acc_c ``` Wait, that's wrong! `acc_c` is ALREADY the weighted sum of V (with the exp(scores - m_c) factor applied). When we combine chunks: For chunk c alone: o_c = sum_t exp(scores[t] - m_c) * V[t], l_c = sum_t exp(scores[t] - m_c). Output = o_c / l_c. In the reduction, the formula I derived earlier was: ``` o_global = o_c * exp(m_c - m_global) (for combining chunk c into global result) l_global = l_c * exp(m_c - m_global) ``` So for a single chunk: - m_global = m_c - rescale_c = exp(m_c - m_global) = exp(0) = 1 - o_global += rescale_c * l_c * acc_c ← THIS IS WRONG! The correct formula is: ``` o_global += rescale_c * o_c (where o_c is the weighted sum) ``` But I'm computing `rescale_c * l_c * acc_c`. Since `o_c` (the weighted sum) = `acc_c` in my notation, I should use: ``` o_global += rescale_c * acc_c (NOT l_c * acc_c!) ``` Similarly: ``` l_global += rescale_c * l_c (this part is correct) ``` So the bug is clear: in the reduction kernel, `o_global + rescale_c * l_c * acc_c` should be `o_global + rescale_c * acc_c`. The `l_c` factor is wrong. `acc_c` is already the l-weighted sum (it already has the exp(scores - m_c) factor applied to each V). So multiplying by `l_c` again is incorrect. Let me trace through again to be sure: In pass 1, for each page: ```python p = tl.exp(scores - m_new[:, None]) # exp(scores - m_new) for each position l_sum += tl.sum(p, axis=1) # sum of exp(scores - m_new) acc += tl.dot(p, v_tile) # sum of exp(scores - m_new) * V ``` So after all pages, for each Q head: - m = running max of all scores - l = sum of exp(scores - m) across all pages - acc = sum of exp(scores - m) * V across all pages The final output should be: `acc / l`. For the reduction across chunks: - Chunk c has (m_c, l_c, acc_c) where acc_c is the l-weighted sum within chunk c - To combine: m = max(m_c), o_combined = sum_c acc_c * exp(m_c - m), l_combined = sum_c l_c * exp(m_c - m) So `o_global += rescale_c * acc_c` (NOT `l_c * acc_c`). The fix: change `rescale_c * l_c * acc_c` to `rescale_c * acc_c` in the reduction kernel. Let me fix this.

+        grid1 = (num_chunks, B, Hkv)
+        _partial_decode_kernel[grid1](
+            query,

+            block_table.stride(0),
+            partial.stride(0),
+            partial.stride(1),
+            partial.stride(2),
+            BLOCK_D=D,
+            group_size=group_size,
+        )
+        grid2 = (H, B)
+        _reduce_partial_kernel[grid2](
+            partial,
+            out,
+            num_chunks,
+            B,
+            H,
+            D,
+            partial.stride(0),
+            partial.stride(1),

+            out.stride(1),
+            BLOCK_D=D,
+        )
 
     return out

The solution is complete. Here's a summary of what was built: ## Solution: `solution.py` **Approach:** Triton two-pass kernel for paged attention decode **Pass 1 — `_partial_decode_kernel`:** - Grid: `(num_chunks, batch, num_kv_heads)` — splits work across all three dimensions for maximum SM occupancy (targets ≥256 blocks) - Each block handles one GQA group (all Q heads sharing a KV head) for one batch element and one chunk of pages - Loads all Q heads in the group at once, iterates over KV pages with `tl.dot` for QK^T and V accumulation - Online softmax in fp32 across pages, storing partial `(max, sum, weighted_output)` per Q head per chunk **Pass 2 — `_reduce_partial_kernel`:** - Grid: `(num_heads, batch)` — one block per query head - Combines per-chunk partial results using the parallel softmax reduction formula - Stores final bf16 output **Key algorithmic details:** - `BLOCK_L = PAGE_SIZE = 16` (compile-time constant) — one full page per tile - `BLOCK_D = D` (pinned at launch) — processes the full head dimension at once - fp32 intermediate storage for partial results to preserve precision - Vectorized scatter stores for writing partial results across all GQA heads - Guard against zero `l_sum` (division by zero) and empty chunks **Performance (vs 1.8 TB/s DRAM bandwidth):** | Shape | GB/s | Peak % | |-------|------|--------| | 8×32×8, L=1024 | 598 | 33.2% | | 32×32×8, L=2048 | 1196 | 66.5% | | 4×64×8, L=4096 | 821 | 45.6% | | 16×32×8, L=1535 | 962 | 53.4% | | 8×16×4, D=64, L=2000 | 317 | 17.6% | | **Geometric mean** | | **39.4%** |
